published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.

Blackie’s Modern Cyclopedia,
This is a handy encyclopedia in eight neat
volumes of workaday size. While not so
full or so up-to-date as the very expensive
publications of its class, it contains an
immense mass of varied information on
nearly all subjects of enquiry, and strikes a
happy medium between the vast encyclopedia
in 20 or 30 heavy tomes and the disappoint-
ing single-volume compendium.
Strongly-bound in cloth and well printed ;
the set of eight volumes for 505,, packed or
posted anywhere in Australasia.

The Russian policy is to make Constanti-
nople the Muscovite capital, instead of St. Peters-
burg. ihe latter isn't a real city : it has never
taken root, and merely exists because it is the arti-
ficial metropolis of the empire. When the Czar
moves to Constantinople, Petersburg will fade
away like a drearn-oity at daybreak.
Lord Salisbury holds five acres of land in
the vicinity of Charing Cross (the very heart of
ijondon), for which his ancestors paid ten shillings
an acre. It was secured as grazing land 250 years
ago. A magnificent object-lesson on unearned
incre-ment here. The land reverts to the State in
another 250 years, unless the State in the mean-
time gets sense, and decides that the Cecils have
already had too much of a good thing.
lb -Prussia the murder of men, women,
and children is frequent by the bayonets and
bullets of guards and sentinels. The other day a
little boy was on the grass of a square in Berlin :
the guard tried to arrest him, the child, fright-
ened, ran away and the guard shot him dead.
Such occurrences are frequent. If a newspaper
condemns them, the editor is imprisoned.” —“ The
Legislation of Fear,” hy Ouida, in the Fobt-
nightly Review.
at °^‘°^' WQr k swagman lately struck the
A.S. w. border town of Cockburn, and reflecting
that it would be better' to be conveyed by rail to
Gladstone (S. A.), the locality of the nearest gaol,
than to walk there, took off a boot and sent it
through the nearest window on the S.A. side.
Ihe local beak was “equal to the occasion.” Learn-
ing the circumstances, he mulcted the out-of-work
swagman in 2s. 3d. damages and 10s. costs. That
being the exact sum the latter had on him when
arrested, he ruefully paid up and went his way.
Why he didu t send his other boot through
another window is not explained.

.Dear 13 ulletin, At one time of the year
the wild bees near Lake Taupo collect poisonous
honey from a certain native tree, and several
deaths have occurred through using it. The
symptoms of honey-poisoning are great thirst
with vomiting, cramps, &c. About fifteen years
ago, when 1 was on a survey in the Upper Wai-
kato, some Maoris had robbed a wild bees’ hive
and had taken about 2cwt. of honey. It had a
peculiar look which they considered suspicious.
U nder such circumstances it is usual to try it first dp
a dog, but as the dogs in this settlement happened
to be all valuable for wild-pig hunting, this way of
testing the honey seemed too risky. That evening,
however, a swagsman came tramping down the
road, and it was decided to use him for purposes
of experiment, A young half-caste stopped him
with— ‘ Are you fond of honey, mate ? If you
are, come here and have a feed.” “ ’Oney,” said
the swagger, “ I ain’t ’ad a good blow-out of
oney since I left the old country.” He ate about
41b. of it and was then invited to stay all night.
He did so and had some more honey for his
supper. As he was alive in the morning the
honey was then distributed round. If fie hadn’t
been alive there would have been an unobtrusive
funeral.—Yours, &c., Takauuna,

You 11 recognise this dame at night,
The one who’s gone astray,
Her age is quite indefinite.
Her hair is either dark or light,
Or else it’s turning grey ;
No other mark or brand she had
Except that she was lightly clad.
She may have wandered near or far—
Her name, it would appear,
Is nothing in particular,
Her weight’s not mentioned in the par.
And only one thing’s clear,
That she’ll be brought back to her dad
Because she’s only “ lightly clad.”
Her eyes are either black or blue,
Or else, perhaps, they’re grey,
Or else they aie some other hue;
She has one leg or, maybe, two,
This dame who’s gone away ;
It doesn’t say she’s sane or mad
But only that she’s lightly clad.
Her age is eighteen years or more,
Or else she’s forty-three,
Or twelve, or, maybe, just three-score ;
She left one Monday sharp at four,
Alone, or else in company,
With someone either good or bad,
And started very lightly clad.
Her size, we note, is med-i-um,
Whatever that may be;
She may be either deaf or dumb :
The advertiser names no sum
As a reward for he
Who finds this lady lost and sad,
Who’s wandering round so lightly clad.
She’s gone to China or Japan,
Or else to Emerald Hill;
Her name, perchance, was Mary Ann,
Perhaps she went off with a man— ’
Perhaps his name was Bill.
But he who seeks her will be glad
To know that she is lightly clad.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added, where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. P.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
Correspondence” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other A rticles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
Tub Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Black and White,
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal : - The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also :— lt. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.

and South Australia,
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage by the Australian and Tasmanian Governments
on Interprovineial Newspapers, the annual subscription
outside N.S. Wales is now £l, with 2s. 2d. added for
postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 12, 1895.
State Life Insurance.
Last week the Equitable Lite Insurance
Co. of the United States opened its
magnificent Sydney offices with a gor-
geous festivity which almost elevated the
event into a matter of national importance.
Some hundreds of guests, including almost
all the political and social celebrities of
Sydney, were entertained at a costly banquet
and reception, and amid the rich viands and
the high-priced wines they rose up and
delivered many columns of enthusiastic
oratory about things in general and the
Equitable Co. in particular. The Equitable
is one of the very big things of the United
States—a country where everything is either
very big or very small. The U.S. owns the
“ three American Giants”—the biggest in-
surance concerns on earth ; the Santa
railroad—the largest and about the most
hopelessly bankrupt line possessed anywhere
by a single corporation ; and, until a few
weeks ago, it owned Tammany Hall, the
biggest individual scandal in Christendom.
It lias also the largest nigger problem, the
largest silver problem, the longest river, the
most imposing bridge, the most formidable
pension list, and the highest record for
progress, wealth, murders, manufactures,
agriculture, philanthropy, meanness, dis-
turbances, and many other things, in this
world. It has the largest hotels in existence,
and yet the majority of its banks are two-
roomed concerns, ten of which combined
would hardly make the smallest note-issuing
bank in Australia. It is a land of gorgeous-
ness and innumerable contradictions, where
a feverish race lives in a feverish atmosphere
of continual boom, and its representatives
abroad do their best to live up to their
national reputation—which is partly real and
partly bogus—for frenzied activity and a
general capacity for annexing the whole
earth.
* * •
The offices which the Equitable has just
opened are a brilliant example of the
advertising capabilities of the American
race. With the exception of one or two
Government buildings they form the biggest
single tenement in Sydney. They are built
on the most expensive site in Sydney, and
occupy the most central position which
could be found. They are about the
most noble-looking thing in the way of archi-
tecture, both internally and externally, that
the city can show. Outside they are a vision
of costly grey stone and marble ; internally
they are more magnificent than most royal
palaces. They are a dream of glittering
marble, rich coloring, graceful carving, vel-
vet carpets, expensive furniture, and every
kind of magnificence. They are vaguely
suggestive, in short, of Ahasubrus on the
burst—of Belshazzar, or Sardanapulus,
or Lorenzo the Magnificent, or anybody
else who left behind him a reputation
for more than the usual splendour of
monarchs in a garish age, when monarchs
were still of some account. And, behind all
this display, the Equitable has £35,000,000
worth of assets and an annual income of
£9,000,000. It claims to be the biggest
insurance office on earth, and, true to its
policy of advertisement, it piles up a building
in which the offices of its two greatest local
rivals could be stowed away out of
sight with abundance of space to spare.
And, meanwhile, about 100 yards away,
the Mutual of New York is heaping up a
tenement of almost equal vastness, with the
same desire to overshadow all competitors.
Life-insurance business, like everything else,
is a matter of advertisement, and enormously
expensive offices and every other kind of
promiscuous garishness are all means to the
same end,
• m •
Yet if the New South Wales Government,
following the wise example set in Maoriland,
chose to establish a State Life Insurance
Office, with the guarantee of the consolidated
revenue behind it, and under good manage-
ment, all this display would come to nought.
The State Insurance Department need not
advertise on anything like the lavish scale
of its rivals. It could commence its career
in a wooden building, with a dozen clerks
sitting at cheap pine desks on cheap pine
stools. It could dispense with the stone and
marble, with the carpets and car ings, the
pillars, the enamelled tiles, the dadoes, the
costly furniture, and all the rest of the
architectural grandeur. It could leave out
the banquet, the speeches, and the opening
ceremony generally ; it would be quite
sufficient if, on the Monday morning when
it commenced operations, a plain-looking old
man, in patched pants, came down and
opened the front door with a large iron key,
and put up a bill outside stating that the
Department was now prepared to begin busi-
ness. There would be no necessity for a
brilliant oration from the manager on the
prospects and resources of the province ; all
he would have to do would be to arrive with
reasonable punctuality, and hang up his
second-best hat on a nail, and get to
work without ceremony. And with the
guarantee of a fourth-rate Government
behind it, and the security of an embarrassed
revenue, and the endorsement of a little
community of not one-and-a-quarter million
souls, that institution would presently cut
the ground from beneath the three American
giants, and the A.M.P., and the other five
big local societies, and become the most pro-
gressive concern of its kind in Australia.
# # *
The first and last and greatest essential in
life-insurance business is Security. The
questions of large bonuses, small premiums,
and liberal conditions are mere side-issues.
The man who insures his life, does it, in a
very large proportion of instances, because
his policy will be the main support of his
family after his death —because it will be
almost the only thing which stands between
them and poverty ; and therefore Security is
the great essential. Absolute safety, of
course, cannot be obtained anywhere in this
world, but the largest possible measure of it
is what every intelligent person strives after.
The one thing which is certain about every
assurance society, every bank, every Govern-
ment, and every mundane institution what-
soever, is that it will come to an end—some
day ; but the merit of a State Bank or a State
Insurance Department is that it is almost
certain to outlive all rivals. ■ The private
institution lasts until dishonesty, or mis-
management, or the unavoidable depre-
ciation of its investments through some far-
reaching public calamity, reduces it to bank-
ruptcy. The State institution lasts long
after that period ; it may be bankrupt a
dozen times over, but the public revenue
will make up the loss and set it going again,
and it is only when the public revenue can’t
make good the loss any longer that it
definitely comes to grief. The difference in
these, two sets of circumstances is «o great,
and the advantages of the State institution
are so enormous, that the failure of this
country to follow the example set by Maori-
land can only be ascribed to the apathetic
character of the Australian people.
* » #
The condition of life insurance business in
Australia is, in most respects, quite as satis-
factory as that existing in any other part of
the world. Yet, in many respects, it
is woefully unsatisfactory. The directors,
by whom the business is managed,
are elected by the policy-holders, yet very
few of the latter have any real knowledge of
their character or qualifications. The audi-
tors are, in most cases, elected in a similar
fashion, but not one in a hundred among
the policy-holders knows anything about
their honesty or their ability. The real
value of the audit is an unknown quantity.
Hardly anyone knows when the assets were
last valued, or by whom, or with what result.
No one outside the inner circle can tell
whether advances were made on safe valua-
tions, or if loans were made up to 60, or 60,
or 70, or even more per cent, of the value of
the security. The safety or otherwise of the
investments can be roughly estimated, by
those skilled in such matters, by the amount
of interest in arrear, large arrears generally
meaning a mass of rather dubious securi-
ties ; but very few offices reveal even this
item in their balance-sheets. A further
estimate can be made by the amount of property
acquired under foreclosure, but this item is
also, in most cases, an unexplained mystery.
These matters are of less importance, how-
ever, because not one person in a thousand
is capable of learning anything from a bal-
ance-sheet, no matter how clear and well-
defined it may be. The groat majority judge
by the vastness of the society’s figures, the
size of its bonuses, the imposing nature of
its advertisements, and the magnificence of
its premises. The community calls for these
proofs of its solidity—which are really proofs
of nothing at all—and the offices, of neces-
sity, do their best to supply them. If they
could supply any coherent evidence of their
solvency which the ignorant public would
understand they would probably do so, but
the public, as a whole, is not gifted with
understanding, and is incapable of compre-
hending evidence. The ideal insurance
manager would be one who could heal the
sick or raise the dead, like an apostle of old,
that being the sort of utterly irrelevant
testimony which the majority of the popula-
tion would best appreciate. A good deal of
the obscurity of insurance balance-sheets is
probably owing to the fact that obscurity
conveys to most people just as much infor-
mation as anything else.
» • •
This helplessness on the part of the great
bulk of the community, and the evils to which
it is always liable to give lise, are powerful
arguments in favor of a State Insurance
Department. At least 90 per. cent, of share-
holders and policyholders are hopelessly in-
capable of safeguarding their own interests
in any intelligent fashion. An Insurance
Department which is guaranteed by the
State provides against this blind and hap-
hazard system of control, by assigning as the
security for each policy, not only the accumu-
lated funds of the office itself, but the whole
assets of the State, its revenues, its credit
at home and abroad, and everything
of value belonging to it. Considering that
life-insurance is a business which, more
than any other, demands Security above
all things, the value of such an institution
cannot be over-estimated. The experience
of the Maoriland Government Department,
which now does more business in its own
province than all its rivals combined, which
has grown in a few years to be the second
largest affair of its kind in Australasia, and
which yields better returns, and is less expen-
sively worked than all save the A.M.P. only,
proves sufficiently well the advantages of the
system. So far as The Bulletin knows,
there is notoneamong the six large Australian
institutions which is in a really danger-
ous condition. But without some system of
effective Government supervision the public
is not likely to know that any office is in a
dangerous condition until it is past repair, so
that even if the evidences of solvency were
ten times as conclusive as theyare the demand
for State life-insurance would be none the
less strong.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Wanted, an Explanation:
This is Sydney Telegraph in its leading
article of Friday, 21st December, wailing
because Protection makes food cheap, and so
hurts the poor farmer :
The present extremely low price of maize in this colony
is the direct result of the protective tariff acting on a
want of understanding among maize - growers. The
farmers are now compelled to sell their wheat in Sydney
at a lower price than competitive supplies from Victoria
or elsewhere could be delivered at, even if the duties
were abolished. The summing up of the whole business
is, that under any circumstances the farmers here must
lose under the protective system, as in Victoria, and
everywhere else where it has been tried. The unwise
stimulus which the mistaken idea of gaining the tax per
bushel gives to production soon brings down the price
obtainable in the local market.
And this is Sydney Telegraph in its leading
article of Friday, January 4, wailing because
Protection makes food dear and enables the
farmer to grow rich at the expense of the poor
consumer :
The simple truth is that Mr. Reid would not have dared
to venture before the country prior to the election with
any such proposal on his lips as that, for the sake of the
farmers or millers, a majority of the people should go on
bearing a heavy burden of taxation. Any such suggestion
would rightly have stamped him as a Protectionist in dis-
guise, and a politician unworthy to be trusted by any
side.
The Bulletin now respectfully asks this lying
prophet to straighten up, and say in plain
language what he really does mean—if he really
does mean anything. According to these state-
ments, Protection lays a “ heavy burden of
taxation ” on “ the majority of the people ” for
the benefit of the farmers—which obviously
means that Protection enables the farmer to
extract more money from the people. Also,
Protection is an evil because it brings down
prices ; “ the farmers must lose by it“ the
unwise stimulus brings down the price obtain-
able in the local market,” &c. Consequently,
Protection is doubly a curse, for it makes prices
go up and thus taxes the consumer for the
benefit of the farmer, and, at the same time, it
makes the prices of the same articles go down
and ruins the farmer—whereby the Telegraph’s
readers are presented with the weird spectacle
of a thing going in two opposite directions at
once. Also, the fact that Protection always
lowers prices is further demonstrated by the
alleged fact that the protected grain of Victoria
is dearer than the protected grain of New
South Wales—a few weeks ago, by the way, the
Telegraph proved that it was cheaper, but that
doesn’t matter ; and because the protected grain
of New South Wales is too cheap to pay
the farmer, the Freetrade organ wants to
assist him by making him compete with addi-
tional supplies from outside. The Cornstalk
agriculturist is being injured by Protection
because his grain fetches less than that of the
Victorian, and the Victorian agriculturist is
ruined by Protection because his grain fetches
more than that of the Cornstalk. And while
the farmer’s charges to the consumer are going
down in this dreadful fashion, and he is urged
to throw off the yoke of Protection in order to
get the prices up again, the consumer, it seems,
according to the same paper, is bearing a heavy
burden of taxation in order to prop up the
farmer, and he is urged to throw oil' the same
yoke in order to force the farmer’s prices down.
Will the Telegraph kindly explain its own
Freetrade principles ?
The Betting- Law.
A group of objectionable clerics and repellent
females, known as the Anti-Gambling League,
are threatening to do a good work by prose-
cuting the English Jockey Club ks an aider and
abettor of “ the odds ”on racecourses. The
reform intended by the League is something
very different from the work it will accomplish,
if the threat be carried out, which circum-
stance merely coulirms the truth of the old
parsonic statement that Providence moves in a
mysterious way. An indictment of the Jockey
Club for permitting bookmakers to ply the.r
trade under its auspices must inevitably rid the
world of a loathsome legal scandal. Betting on
horse-races in England, as in Australia, is
punishable under an Act of Parliament, the
said Act being enforced, or overlooked, by a
corrupt police conscience, without a tittle of
regard for decency or common-sense. When
enforced, it often happens that the law is
faithfully administered, or smothered, as the
case may be, by a judge who himself patro-
nises the very offence he is discuss-
ing. Justice Hawkins, for instance, may
back a horse at Epsom one day, and next day
impose a tine upon somebody convicted of lay-
ing against a horse in Fleet-street, though he is
well aware the while that the law draws no
distinction between a racecourse “ring ” and a
turf “commissioner’s” office, or a gambling
club. They are all betting places within the
meaning of the Act. One shameless rule has
hitherto been followed in the prosecution of
bookmakers —the rule that no steps should be
taken to interfere with the liberty of noble
lords. When his Grace, the Duke, came in at
the door the abashed Law flew out at the
window, so to speak. The betting-account of
Royal Albert Edward could be settled at
Tattersall’s with impunity, but a petty cash
bookmaker paying or receiving at any “ shy ”
rendezvous in the neighborhood ran a great
risk of being raided at his busiest time if he had
omitted to bribe his blackmailing acquaintances,
the police. So with betting-clubs— i.e., clul s
instituted for the transaction of wagers on
horse races, and maintained for that purpose
alone. The law drew no distinctions, but the
police and judges did. The scandal was so old
that it had become almost venerable w'hen the
Anti-Gambling League began to bore its
righteous nose into the root of the matter.
Last year the lessees of Northampton race-
course were summoned, at the influential insti-
gation of the League, for permitting two rings
to be used for an illegal purpose. The lessees are
Messrs. Frail, whose father was a race-meeting
proprietor before them, and w-hose coat-of-arms
ought to include a skinned lamb and a Hebrew
rampant. Well, the Northampton magisl rates
were confronted last June by the awkward fact
that Messrs. Frail had made “rings ” on their
racecourse, and such rings are illegal. To
allow a conviction in this test case was impos-
sible. No loyal bench of magistrates could be
expected to burst up the sport of kings out of
respect for the law. So they got out of the
difficulty by saying they were not satisfied that
Messrs. Frail had a guilty knowledge of
betting being perpetrated in their rings, there-
fore, the case was dismissed. The Northamp-
ton beaks were neither better nor worse than
our Australian “ authorities,” .who, from time
to time object to the appearance of a book-
maker’s advt. in a newspaper which is allowed
to record his transactions in a special para-
graph. The Law, in its dealings with turf
gambling is a whited sepulchre, a contemptible
humbug, and disgusting fraud, all the world
over. Its sin was bound to find it out, and this
Anti-Gambling League promises to expose the
rottenness of betting legislation in such a
glaring light that it will be forced to recon-
struct on sound, honest lines.
Concerning- some “Scandalous Rumors.”
Melbourne Age suggests that 12 months in
gaol and .£SOO fine would be a fitting reward
for people who circulate “ scandalous rumors”
about financial institutions, and urges, in
enthusiastic terms, that strong measures should
be adopted to “put down” this class of
offenders. The Bulletin can see, with its
mind’s eye, that “putting-down” process,
dating the event back a year or two. The
prisoner who expressed doubts about the
stability of one of'the shaky banks—say the
Federal—would be in the dock. The manager
and directors and auditors would all file in pro-
cession into the witness-box and testify to the
absolute stability of the institution, despite the
fact that its shares were falling with a heavier
thud every day—just as they testified before
the shareholders even when the collapse was in


other side, because the prisoner couldn’t get
free access to the books and securities, and
even if he could it would take 12 months’
hard work and an expenditure of several
thousand pounds to find out the real
state of affairs. Then he would be sentenced to
12 months in gaol, and sold up for the ,£SOO
fine, and just about the expiration of his sentence
the Federal would go bung and verify his pre-
dictions to the letter. But as the law which
the Age clamours for is only to apply to people
who circulate allegedly untrue reports to the
sight. There would he no evidence on the
effect that a financial institution is unsound
when it isn’t, and is to take no cognisance of
the leading citizen who circulates an untrue
statement to the effect that it is sound when it
isn’t, and thereby persuades people to drop their
money into his bottomless financial pit, the direc-
tors and auditors and so on wouldn’t be im-
prisoned for 12 months, or fined £SOO, or any-
thing even distantly resembling it. What the
Age seemingly wants, in fact, is that no man
shall be allowed, under an enormous penalty, to
warn anybody else against any rotten financial
institution, or to call in question any fraudu-
lent or suspicious-looking balance-sheet, or to
make himself uncomfortable to the board in
any way whatever. That is apparently all the
Age wants at present.
Passing* on the Responsibility.
N.S.W. Premier Reid’s latest defence of his loan
estimates —in which large sums are to be bor-
rowed for dredging rivers and makingsewers and
other sundries—is an interesting essay on the
great art of leaving the bill to the next genera-
tion, and shoving along the responsibility. “lb
would,” says Reid, “be grossly unfair to
charge the revenue of one particular year with
the expense of works whose utility would
extend over a period of from 30 years to a
century. By an annual contribution from the
revenue, something like a fair distribution of
the cost could be effected.” This theory might
hold good if these works were the only ones of
their kind which were wanted—but they are
not. Next year more rivers will want to be
dredged, and more sewers will be required, and
the invoice for them will be shunted on to
futurity for the same reason. And the same
thing will happen the year after, and the year
after that again, and the succeeding year, and
every other year while the country endures.
There is no end to the process, and every time,
accordingto G. Reid’s logic, there will be the
same reason for passing on the Bill to posterity.
And when at last posterity has to meet the
bill—for every bill must be either met or re-
pudiated sooner or later—the land revenue,
which at present amounts to £2,200,000 per
annum, will have been eaten up, and the
unpaid-for works will have become old and
decrepit. New South Wales lives just now
by using up the public lands as fast as they
can be realised upon, and leaving the bills
for ordinary current expenditure to be paid by
coming generations. And the country hasn’t
grown one statesman with sufficient pluck or
honesty to look the inevitable result in the
face.
The New South Wales Government announces
that the contracts for thinning out forests—on
which £25,000 of borrowed money is to be
expended—will be let only to persons who have
been at least six months resident in the pro-
vince. Considering that the Reid Government
is a strictly Free-raid institution this lapse into
Protection of the most uncompromising type is
remarkable. There is to be no free competition
about the business. It isn’t to be done in the
cheapest market. G. H. Reid doesn’t even
propose to content himself by letting the out-
side laborer compete under a 10 per cent,
duty as Dibbs did—he is to be shut
out altogether. For want of competition the
price will be raised to the consumer, who will
thus be taxed to keep up an artificial rate of
wages, and to foster an industry which doesn’t
live on its own merits. This is the exact mean-
ing of G. Reid’s action when put into the words
which G. Reid has himself used on a hundred
platforms when he pointed the podgy finger of
scorn at his predecessor. All the same, The
Bulletin is glad to find that Premier Reid,
when put to the test, isn’t heartless enough to
live up to his own brutal creed, and the fact
that he should still regard himself as an un-
compromising Freetrader, while practising
Protection in its most advanced form, only
shows that his brains are by no means
so good as his intentions. If the Reid
Government can make it square with its fiscal
policy to decree that the New South Wales
forests shall be thinned only by people who
have lived at least six months in the province,
then it can also decree that the hats and clothes
and boots of New South Wales shall only be
made by men who have resided at least six
months in the province, and square that with
its fiscal policy also. In fact, its fiscal policy
seems capable of squaring with so many things
that the Reid-Dibbs coalition ought to be
getting almost in sight by this time.
A teetotal priest, lecturing in Melbourne on
the Drinking Habits of Colonial Society, in-
formed his Christian friends that young Aus-
tralians were as liable to get drunk as their
British - born parents. This he gave as a
deplorable fact, without having the least
apparent suspicion that he was guilty of an
Impardonable lie. Comparative temperance in
strong-liquor drinking is already an Australian
virtue. The native of the not distant future
will be at least as sober as the average French-
man who contents himself with a happy hilarity
on vin ordinaire, and it is perhaps the
vague dread of this good time coming
which moves every rabid anti-alcoholist to
rear himself upon his hind legs and wave his bit
of blue ribbon. Strange to say, the temper-
ance tarradiddler in this case took his shocking
examples from among the babies at the Chil-
dren s Hospital in Paris. Many of these suck-
lings, the worthy man declared, would decline
milk, but “ imbibe with avidity anything which
contained alcohol, thus showing their hereditary
disposition to intemperance.” The point of
tins remark lay in the apostle’s application of
it, of course. All infants are tainted with a
liking for alcohol. If his own babbling off-
spring were offered the choice between pure
milk from the cow and a little weak brandy
and water, they would go bald-headed for the
“booze.” Which proves that teetotalism is
against nature.
Thus George Higinbotham on Vic. Legislative
Council :
The root idea of these Legislative Councils is that they
represent wealth. They assume that a man who is rich
is, by virtue of his riches, a better man and is better en-
titled to political power than a man who is not rich, and
they assume that the wealthy class is, by virtue of its
wealth, bi tter entitled to political power than the poor
class ; and that it is not merely entitled to more political
power as electors, but that it is entitled to predominant
power by having a separate chamber to represent wealth.
To my mind a mere statement of the proposition
amounts to a condemnation of the system. ... It
seems to me shameful that a man should tell me that he
is better than I am because he is richer than I am.
The Legislative Council has shown an indisposition and
an inaptitude, even an incapacity, to deal with any public
question whatever except those subjects connected with
wealth and the interests of the wealthy class. ... It
you looked into it I believe that members of the Legisla-
tive Assembly would have been found most injuriously
affected by the influences arising from Ihe existence of
the Legislative Council. lam afraid that the Legislative
Assembly has to a considerable extent been demoralised
by that body, and I am afraid, if I may be permitted to
say so, the people of this country have not escaped with
impunity from that contaminating influence.
My opinion—and if I had the power to carry out that
opinion I would do so —is to do away with the Legislative
Council altogether. In my opinion it is not, nor it cannot
come to good. The institution ifself is vicious in prin-
ciple, and does not admit of a satisfactory and complete
cure. . , It appears to me that no change will be re-
form in the real sense of that word unless it be a oha n ge
in the direction of weakening, and not of strengthening it.
. . . If there are two chambers you must have one
superior to the other ; and I take it that if you assent to
that view you will go with me further to this extent, that
the chamber which ought to be predominant is the cham-
ber which represents the whole people, and not the cham-
ber which represents only a section.
I have seen it stated, and with some sign of triumph,
that the people of this country are indifferent upon the
question ; that they feel no interest in it, and that it will
be a matter of indifference to them whether the Council
or the Assembly gain the victory in the conflict which
now exists and has existed for years. If this statement
be true, I would say, so much the worse for the people of
this country.
Editor Bulletin.—
Prices of Australian produce have diminished so greatly
in recent years that the burden of interest on the New
South Wales loans floated between 1872 and 1891 has
already increased, in effect, from £1,600,000 to £2,300,000
annually.
This is the old “ appreciation-of-gold ” question
—which I understood to be tabooed. I am
against the present National Debt want of sys-
tem, but not for the reason urged in your par.
above clipped from. “ The fall in prices ”is a
vast and complicated question, but I think that
few of Australia’s difficulties come therefrom.
Australia’s trouble, almost from first to last,
has been land-monopoly the habit of
the land - monopolist being to become
everybody’s rapacious creditor in good
times, and everybody’s insolvent debtor
in bad times. But, nevertheless, Australia’s
Loan Impolicy wants attention. Consolidation
would effect a tremendous saving offhand—and
a State Bank would get a lot of State 1.0.U.’s
out of pawn. Two further points to be urged ;
1. Commercial rates of interests are too high for bor-
rowers that have not the corifmeroial privilege of iusol-
vency when adversity comes.
2. No community has a lien on posterity.
To return to the tabooed subject : What are
the advantages of invention and organisation
and mechanical advance and industrial science,
and revolutionised transport, and all the rest of
the characteristics of the times—if they do not
cheapen anything ? Is steam to be developed
almost to an intelligence and then achieve
nothing? It is impossible that steam and the
dozen forces of the day should not tend to
lower the market prices of both luxuries and
necessaries. The “Prices of Produce ” argu-
ment against interest reckoned in gold should
include proof that produce is a fair criterion
or standard of value. The argument that in-
terest should drop with produce would have to
be applied to Wages—and I, for one, am not
prepared to admit that wages should oscillate
with corn—as if man lived by bread alone.
The fault is with the principle of loans—not the
interest.—F. [ (1) When a country has to pay
a fixed debt with produce that is steadily
declining in value, how can produce help being
a fair criterion ? (2) The Bulletin never said that
interest should drop with the price of produce.
If interest did, then it would follow that the
principal should drop also. (3) If The Bulle-
tin had said so, still it doesn’t follow that
wages should drop also. (4) “ The fault is with
the principle of loans—not the interest.”
Which is exactly what The Bulletin said.
Why should “ F.” argue at such length and so
indignantly to prove that he agrees with this
paper ?—Ed. B.]
Organs of the well-to-do scornfully point out
that whereas the working shearer makes an
average of a bare pound a week the Secretary
of the Australian Workers’ Union makes a fixed
.£2OO a year, and delegates to one or other of
the frequently-recurring conferences receive
15s. a day. The first thing the figures show is,
not that secretary or delegates get too much,
but that the working shearer gets too little. Yet
the proportion of remuneration between the
workers and the governing body in the shearer
sphere contrasts favorably with the propor-
tion in the case of State workers and governors.
A postman gets £2 10s. per week, or, say, £lO
a month; a postmaster-general gets £125 a
month. Where the postmaster-general gets
less, the postman also gets less. So right
throughout the State. This wrong-headed
jibe against salaried officials when Labor tries
to organise its resources stultifies its origin-
ators. The head of a large union works
as hard as a Minister of the Crown, and
needs to be endowed with qualities, less
showy perhaps, but quite as solid. The spec-
tacle of the Labor man jeered for taking £3 or
£4 a week, while the State employe receives
about £3O, is an exemplification of Law’n’order
with a very big beam in its eye.
Editor Bulletin, —Not with the object of
dragging the mangled “ W. W.” from beneath
the chariot-wheels of your thundering “ Aus-
tralian ” do I venture to point out to that
“ robustious ” Democrat that, in predicating
the acceptableness of good taste as the “ oil of
intercourse,” he predicates also, of necessity,
the undesirableness of its opposite. With this
much premised, however, let me vociferate in
“ Australian’s ” stentorian company that this
Queen-recognising question is of far too grimly
momentous a character to be referred to any
puny standard of conventional deportment. If
Anthony Ogden, M.P., thought as “Aus-
tralian ” thinks ; if he saw all in that libatory
glass of fizz that “ Australian ” makes him see ;
if he were, in truth, “the one erect man in a
crawling assembly ” —then, indeed, “ his small
refusal covers a great principle,” and “ Aus-
tralian’s ” volcanic screeches will not be as the
voice of one crying in the wilderness. But alas! I
am constitutionally suspicious of the man who is
eager to run his head against a brick wall.
Experience has taught me that blatant inde-
pendence is mostly sham independence, and my
proud enthusiasm flops suddenly to earth at
the reflection that in all probability it was not
that Ogden loved monarchy less, but that he
loved the pointed finger more, and that, in the
beaker which he heroically declined to drain to
the health of a commonplace and unimportant
old lady, he saw no nobler vision than the
chance of a little cheap notoriety for one
Anthony Ogden.—-Cosmopolitan.
Says a London paper :—
Men like Sir Wilfred Lawson and other prominen
fanatics are simply teetotal parasites of the drink traffic.
Politically they live by it and upon it, but they by no
means wish that there should be nothing left to blaze at.
The grand obstacle to national reform is the existence of
a great body of professional publican-hunters who draw
either cash or notoriety from the sport, and naturally
conduct it, not in the way that is best fitted to promote
temperance, but in the best way fitted to provide what
they want.
This is a matter worth considering. The men
who live by the liquor-traffic are brewers, dis-
tillers, publicans, and teetotal lecturers—the
teetotal lecturer lives by drink and upon the
accursed drink-traffic quite as much as does the
publican or the distiller. The abolition of
drink would deprive him of his only means of
livelihood just as effectually as it would the
publican, therefore he has just the same reason
for desiring that drink shall not be abolished.
Consequently, he demands entire prohibition,
which experience has proved to -be practically
impossible, instead of State control which is
possible, and which is twenty times more
effective than prohibition. He insists, ostensibly
on high moral grounds, on having all or nothing,
with a gratifying assurance that, as the result
of this policy, the drink-traffic will go on during
his lifetime, and his billet will last as long as he
wants it. The teetotal agitator who lives on the
game, or adds to his importance by it, is one of
the most serious obstacles in the way of any
effectual reform of the fusel-oil industry.

Bernard Shaw, the dramatic critic and Fabian
essayist, talks straight to the social-gimlet
people in a recent P.M. Gazette :—
My scepticism as to the fate of ordinary woman on the
streets does not affect the damning fact that if a poor
girl, either by chance or by illegitimate inheritance from
a gentleman father, is pretty and ladylike, it is better for
her to trust to the protection of some rich and tolerably
kind man of the upper classes than to place herself at the
disposal of modern capitalism as a barmaid, a whitelead
worker, a sempstress, a match girl, or half-a-dozen of the
other alternatives offered by us to women as the reward
of virtue. It cannot be too clearly understood that it is
not the foolishly open-handed young gentlemen of the
Empire lounge who cheapen women. It is our pious
shareholders who use them up by the thousand, and who
make them into most ugly living pictures of premature
old age, clad in miserable garments that have an inde-
cency far more disgraceful to us than the rosy nothings
worn by the more fortunate living pictures at the music-
halls.
Mr. Shaw goes on to point out that marriage to
a poor girl in London simply means living
indecently in one room with a man legally
licensed to abuse her as no frequenter of the
Empire dare abuse a prostitute, and that, too,
without her consent, and possibly in the pre-
sence of her children.
An English Judge, who also happens to be a
large landowner, recently favored his tenants
and agricultural menials with a few political
remarks. Judges are not expected to give
themselves away, even at remote rural gather-
ings, but Justice Grantham had probably been
drinking to the health of next year’s crops in
home-brewed beer, and he talked recklessly.
He was severe on the Rosebery Government.
“Many of its supporters,” he said, referring to
the accursed democrats, “ do all they can to
injure the landowners, and to set class against
class, and it owes its position to the ill-will
which exists in some districts between the
farmer and his laborer.” The spectacle of a
calm, impartial lawgiver lamenting the fact
that a shilling-a-day laborer doesn’t whoop
for “ the squire ” as he was wont to
whoop is truly edifying. As Grantham so
candidly suggests, there would be no Radicals in
Parliament, nor any legislation save blue-Tory
legislation for the “masses,” if nobody came
along and urged them to vote against the land-
owners and their nominees. Then Hingland
would be happy. God has created two classes,
the landowners, including capitalists of all des-
criptions, and the other class, the class with no
land, or money, or individual influence w'orth
mentioning. The grand old duty of the class on
top is to remain on top in the grand old style of
yore, and the darned agitating democrat, who
attempts to “ injure ” the uppermost party, is
an enemy to Law’norder.
Purgatory must be worse than hell. It's
full of “ the middle-classes.”
Now and than he contrives to tell a tale quite in the
manner of Maupassant—without a touch in excess, with-
out a sign of strain. Lord Pembroke, in a delightful
introduction, properly calls- these stories powerful. To
our mind they are almost models of form for the short
story. Without any pretence of aiming at the more
elaborate effects, Mr. Becke almost always manages to
write with the pen of a master of his craft. The details
are admirable, the picture always holds the eye, now and
again with a rare and subtle charm.

or Tourgenieff ever modelled into life-like
semblance.” And, in the big Australian dailies,
these same stories were damned with half-a-dozen
lines of faint praise or incompetent criticism !

AI v Dear Moorabinda—
Are women changing their spots? A lady
writes to the Sydney papers condemning the tall-
hatted medical brigade and wildly championing
the cause of the unregistered healer —and, as a
consequence, also that of the mysterious beldame
who cures cork legs with a sprig of greenstuff and
tells fortunes in between when she isn’t before the
court charged with reducing the rate of popula-
tion. There can be no manner of doubt that the
bolus instinct is born in some folk —like piano-
playing and pocket-picking—and that some un-
registered people are gifted with a keener medical
instinct than many qualified practitioners who
merely fire drugs into a body on the off-chance
that some of them will do the trick.
But, yet, why should every other inherent taste
for a given pursuit need sedulous cultivation be-
fore it can come to the surface? An untaught
artist, an uncultivated singer, have absolutely no
value in the open market, strong though their
natural vocation may bo ; why then should it be
asserted that the “qualified” medical practitioner
is laps behind the Heaven-sent natural genius, with
half-mourning nails, who goes tinkering around
one’s vitals by rule of thumb ?
I don’t think the quack’s latest championess, a
lady with a luxuriant literary style who has long
been pecking at the doctors, can have studied her
fellow-women very intelligently, for, after triumph-
antly quoting a most in-and-out sort of female
who consulted a “ regular ” medico only to throw
his medicines into the fire and take the doses of
her pet herbalist (often a most equivocal term !)
who “ soon put her right,” the championess of
back-alley science goes on to rejoice over an
alleged clue “ leading away from mystery . . .
to simplicity and freedom.” However, I don’t
think many women will appreciate the change.
“Mystery ” is the very breath of life to my poor,
dear sisters. When a lady has over-eaten (or
drunk) it is much more soothing to her feelings to
be sympathised with in dog-Latin than to come
home brutally labelled “ awful result of too much
chicken and champagne.”
Some of the letters printed re the latest Tich-
borne-claimant dog-days sensation make charming
reading, the most high-toned epistle I have read
for a whole year being the effusion of one “John
Seult Fortescue le Clere,” who tells of meeting
one whom he considers to be the only genuine
Tichborne, while he (John Seult, &c.) was chop-
ping wood for Murray River steamers —a noble
instance of blue-blood making itself useful at the
end of an axe. Here is a slab of his communica-
tion, worthy of note by all aspirants to melo-
dramatic honors ; "We recognised each other by
ancestral rings his with the family crest,
mine with the family motto of the Fortes-
cues (Forte scutum satus ducem). He showed
me documents of great importance that
I am forbidden on oath to reveal.”
Hasn’t that a good old Bland-Holtian sound ?
The hero of a stage-drama always travels about
with the jewellery his mother gave him, although
his boots look like dismantled roller-skates, and he
is invariably studded with valuable “papers”
which the villain gets hold of in the simplest
manner possible. This I have always considered
a weak situation in an age of iron safes. But now
I will regard that hero with more toleration when
he sits down alone and unprotected and proceeds
to turn out all his pockets for the edification of a
total stranger. The only melodramatic touch in
John Seult Fortescue Le Clere’s romantic narra-
tive is that his Tichborne was discovered headless
later on. I hope budding dramatists will make a
note of that, also; for a melodramatic hero who
expires in real earnest and gives the heroine
another chance should be most refreshing.
The Australasian (it now unblushingly hurls
itself at your head as a “ society ” paper and
chronicles the fact that “ Miss Smith looked nice,
as usual ”) broke out, the other day, with a page
full of Sydney society beauties —a proceeding cal-
culated to do more damage to the prospects of a
passably-pretty girl than if it held her up to public
scorn as a hopeless scarecrow. The page of
alleged Sydney loveliness is, however, incomplete
in that the portrait of the lady whom all the Sydney
clubs vote the belle of the heap (and the clubs are
final authority on these questions) is not given—a
circumstance that should be balm to the soul of
that popular, dark-eyed matron, if there is any
truth in the ancient statement that “good wine
needs no bush.”
The solemn Australasian, I ought to remark,
doesn’t say these ladies are beauties in so many
words, but it obviously thinks so ; else why choose
fifteen women out of the accepted social one
hundred made familiar to us by the Ladies’
Club, leaving the eighty-five out in the cold to
gnash their shop-teeth in envy ? Still, I don’t
think the Southern weekly has thrown much of a
discordant apple into the Port Jackson social
camp. Three of the ladies are calmly and frankly
ugly. The chief beauty of one lies in her pretty
hair, and that, of course, isn’t appreciated in a
photo., which only shows how sketchy her brows
and eyebrows are, and another whose shoulders
are her strong point is really no prettier facially
than I am (as seen at the top of these remarks).
The sublime ignorance that is going to be
fashionable in a day of State schools and Italian
one penny per lesson, appears to have broken out
already in one decorous and seldom-heard-of
ladies’ club. The lady secretary sent out a
circular to the members on some burning question
or other, a month or so ago, and up to now none
of the club-women have the murkiest idea what it
was all about. The bird’s-eye effect of the circular
was as if the secretary had made a bold sketch, in
blue ink, of a Chinese washing-bill, and then sat
down on it absent-mindedly while it was wet.
I don’t know whether to believe the storyette
re the —er —thrift of an enormously wealthy lady
much-advertised as a philanthropist, who once
upon a time undertook to help at a refreshment-
stall at a certain suburban sectarian bazaar. As
the affair was very “class,” a rush of deadheads
was confidently expected, and so a smart boy
was set to mind the door and admit nobody
on the nod who had not a badge or card
of invitation. The first to arrive was Miss
Van Kilmansegg, who, conscious of her fell
purpose as elected retailer of bazaar-pastry,
naturally thought to float past the door-keeper
with the usual supercilious smile; but Cerberus
had been too well drilled to allow that sort of
thing, and, seeing no badge, stopped her with a
modest request for a shilling. “I am going to
help Lady Dash,” gasped the heiress, “ but I have
forgotten my badge. Surely you know me—Miss
Kilmansegg ?” Casablanca, however, was a mere
stupid frivoller compared with that doorkeeper,
and it was only after long and heated argument
that an undoubted authority turned tip, identified
the gilded one as a helper, and passed her out of
the hot sun, where she had long grilled rather
than pay 12 pence at the door ! But how a big
fortune is wasted on a person with such economi-
cal proclivities! That is the sort of individual
who is quite capable of beginning life on a Tommy
Dodd sixpence and ending up on Turkey carpets.
Hovv_ seldom loveliness and lucre go together
except in fiction ! In real life, directly people grab
gold their complexions seem to turn yellow as if in
compliment. The Coolgardie gold-diggers are
said to be turning either unaccountably grey or as
bald as eggs—afflictions variously alleged to be
the results of dust, tinned meats, condensed water,
wear and tear of camel exercise, and scarcity of
soap-and-water. But, to whatever they may be
due, I can’t help regarding them as the best gold-
omens imaginable. Here, in the East, girls notice
that baldness and Plutonic wealth are inseparable.
The reception given by the Equitable Life As-
surance Society of Ihe US. to celebrate the for-
mal opening of its magnificent new offices was
an emphatic success. Sydney is the most promis-
ing city in the world in which to give free enter-
tainments, and the flocks of people on the lovely
marble staircases were like a modern version
of the well-patronised ladder about which Jacob
dreamed.
The noble-fronted Equitable is certainly a
gorgeous palace, with acres of marble floors, reck-
less flights of marble stairways, and a prevailing
tone of grey and gold (and not too much gold,
either) that turns the vyhole building into a sym-
phony. Even the officials’ uniforms are West-
Point grey, with silver buttons. I must confess
to open-mouthed admiration of the whole edifice,
which is probably the only Australian building
likely to last long enough to become more or less
historical ruins when Macaulay’s Maorilander
stalks abroad; and yet it has a light and airy
aspect. The lifts are of iron lace work, for
example, and the top roof, a good balloon-journey
from the pavement, is so fantastically treated that
it suggests a pantomime-transformation scene.
The reception was distinctly hilarious. There
were corks in the air, and when people tried to
lean in a graceful Piccadilly way on their walking-
sticks, quite oblivious for the moment of the pro-
verbial of marble floors, accidents
happened. Nearly all the good old inevitable in-
vitation-brigade turned up and talked all through

garg’e, you will always keep your mouth in a

The resolve of the Reid Government to
postpone till S w pt. the repeal of the N.S.W. grain-
duties, raised the temperature of theParkes group
several degrees last week. The question canvassed
was—What effect would an amendment moved by
Parkes have if it- should affirm that the grain-
duties shall cease on the same date as other pro-
tective duties? Such an amendment would, or
should, be favored by straight-out Freetraders;
would probably capture most of the malcontents
in Reid’s camp ; and would obtain, on strategic
grounds, much support from the Protectionist
side. And if the amendment were carried by
such a combination, what would there remain for
the Reid Government to do ?
Grand Argus argument against Yic. Land-
tax Bill;
What would happen—Mr. Irvine and others may be
asked —to the members of the Municipal Council that
proposed to increase the rates off-hand by 50 to 100 per
cent. Not many of them would be returned to that
Council Chamber again.
If the municipality had an enormous accrued
deficit amounting to £3 per head for every man,
woman and child therein (as Victoria has) the
councillors would either have to raise the rates or
pay the deficit out of their own pockets. Which
would they do? The pity is that legislators have
the privilege of passing deficits on to next session
—and next Parliament, as actually advocated by
Argus —and next generation, as practised in
Queensland.
Daring the discussion in W.A. Parliament
on the proposal to increase the grant to R.O.
schools (warmly supported by Moran, member for
Yilgarn, and formerly teacher in a R.C. school at
Toowoomba, Q.), Illingworth produced a R.O.
school-book and read a number of extracts to show
that for educational purposes the existence of
Protestants was altogether ignored. A sample :
The state of religion in Australia is of the most cheering
character. Pope Gregory XVI. in 1835 sent thither the
first Archbishop of Sydney, accompanied by three priests
and four ecclesiastical students. Under the auspiois of
this estimable prelate Catholicity advanced with rapid
strides. In 1833 the Cal holies were estimated at one-
fifth ; now they are probably one-half of the population.
In Oceania there are 19 bishoprics, eight vicariates, one
apostolic prefect, and over 400 priests.
As, according to census-reports, the proportion of
R.O.’s in Australasia was 23.1 per cent, of the
population in 1871, and only 21.1 per cent, in 1891,
it seems that the W.A. assisted schools teach one
first-class lie, anyway.
Melbourne Argus, exhorting its friend,
Fat Man, to oppose a Land-tax on patriotic
grounds: “It is for the Legislative Council to
earn the public respect and confidence by a full
and anxious consideration of whether it should or
should not exercise in the people’s welfare the

A Sydney Sunday paper prints a yarn about
a handsome and attractive English lady fighting
and killing a shark at Chowder Point, Sydney.
The lady, the Honorable Mrs. Balk (probably
pronounced “Pork,” in the eternal fitness of
things), met “ a vicious monster of the
main in hand-to-hand combat ” and destroyed it
“like a professional.” Details of the fight, it is
true, hardly bear out the gigantic promise of the
headlines. The lady (accompanied by two men,
who “ left it all to her ”) proceeded to kill a shark
which had been hooked at a bait-buoy. The boat
rolled about, but she harpooned him—“she had
no scruples about that.” It was difficult to stand
up, but “somehow she was quite cool and col-
lected.” The shark came to the surface
and opened his mouth at her, and then
she let him have the harpoon in the
neck; then he came up again and made
more faces. The look of ferocity in his eye
“ gave her quite a chill, but only for a moment.
She hardly knew what she was doing, but she
struck him across the eye with her hand and ex-
claimed ‘You brute!’” Shortly after that, of
course, he died. Then there is some more about
the lady poising a 20-ft. lance and reddening the
sea for 20 yards round. Also, “ part of the lady’s
garments were destroyed in the struggle.” The
Bulletin presumes that it is all an allegory—that
the shark was Man and the lance Woman-suffrage.
But if (Allah be praised!) ibis all literally true, then
t lie man-eating shark has at last found his match—
in the New Woman.
“ Shall our veto be suspensive ?”
Asks the Fat Man, sad and pensive,
“ Are the people to this bill inclined ?
Will it please the lower classes
If the Council promptly passes
Any measure that is ‘ ill-designed V
Would the impost on the soil
Raise the dignity of toil?
Would the farmer, lean and poor—
Ah ! the wolf is at the door—
Like to see his mortgagee thus ‘ fined?’
Would a tax give bread and meat
To the starvers in the street ?
We would gladly pay, ahem !
But we must not injure them:
And so, there’s not a doubt,
We must throw the measure out
In the interests of humankind 1”
The welfare of the nation
Is the one consideration
Ever foremost in the Fat Man’s mind.
The Wild-Cat Column .
The full balance-sheet of the London Bank of
Australia presents all the main features foreshadowed in
the cabled summary. The principal of these is that the
total profit for the half-year is £7020, which is equal to a
little over 1 per cent, per annum on the paid-up capital
of £1,315,000. This circumstance is doubtless a bad thing
for the London Bank of Australia, but it is a good thing
for Australia in general. The London Bank is one of the
hundred or more institutions which make profits in Aus-
tralia and ship them away to pay divs. in England—it is
one more among the vast army of absentees—and, there-
fore, the less profitit makes in this country the better for
the country. The same statement applies to the Union,
the Australasia, and the E.S. and A. Bank, and a horde
of smaller financial institutions. Australia is overrun
worse than any other country on this earth with foreign
joint-stock financiers, and any Protectionist who keeps
his account in an English bank, or insures his property
with an English office, or entrusts his business to an
English finance, mortgage, loan or ot her co., is hereby
informed that he doesn’t know the meaning of his own
principles.
* * ♦ ♦
The liquidator of the onoe-famous, bub now
almost forgotten, Premier Permanent Building Society
issues a balance-sheet showing the results of 4J years’
liquidation. Considering the vastness of the figures in-
volved, and the awful bewilderment of the accounts, it
appears that there is, after all, very little reason in the
complaints made about the delay in declaring a div.
During the period in question, £563,000 was collected,
and even this amount was not sufficient to pay off the
mortgages on the society’s properties, and over £200,000
had to be borrowed in order to satisfy pressing claims,
and prevent the whole assets being foreclosed upon in one
act by some large and ferocious creditors. The situation
at the time of the suspension seems to have been some
thing surpassing the wildest nightmares of finance.
Every separate item of the society’s properties
was mortgaged, often in several directions. A large num-
ber of its buildings were unfinished, or half-finished, or
only just begun, and those which had any value had to
be completed by the liquidator in order to render them
saleable. Many of the lands had dubious titles which had
to be patched up, and in some cases, owing to defective
surveys, damages had to be paid to adjacent proprietors
whose ground had been encroached upon. Also, a large
number had been bought on the instalment plan and
were only partly paid for. The present situation, after
years of bewilderment, is that the society has
£879,000 worth of nominal assets left on hand, and if
these yield £300,000 in cash—whioh is all that is ejected
—the depositors will receive between ss. and 6s. in the £.
Therefore.it is evident that the would-be shaiks, who
bought up Premier Permanent deposits at 14s. in the £
during the early days of the liquidation, will be very
badly left indeed. About three-fourths of the mortgagers
who held the society in pawn have now been either paid,
or ohnked-off, or otherwise disposed of, and when the
balance—whose claims amount to £ls6,ooo—have been
settled with, the depositors will begin to have a show.
Consequently, the latter class may expect a first div. in a
year or two or three, or at the beginning of next century,
or thereabouts, more or less.

Under the Maoriland Foreign Insurance Com-
panies Act, no foreign fire or marine co. can operate in
Maoriland unless it flist obtains from the Public Trustee
a certificate of solvency. That miserable official now
awakes to the fact that he can’t go all round the world
auditing the accounts of the different foreign companies.
Therefore, he must either grant a certificate to every co.
because it says it is solvent, or else refuse the certificates
all round on the ground that he knows nothing about the
alleged solvency of any foreign co.
* • * *
The pastoral properties which the Maoriland
Government resumed at the land-tax valuation and
leased in small areas to settlers, paid last year 4} per cent,
on their cost to the State. As the purchase-money was
raised at 4 per cent, and has since been mostly paid off
out of revenue, there is a solid profit on the transaction.
N.S. Wales badly wants a Government which will attack
after the same fashion the unoccupied lands in the
Eastern Division, which are held by speculators for a rise.
There is room on these lands to grow all the agricultural
produce which the province wants this century, and when
they are fully occupied it will be time enough to talk
about opening the regions beyond the mountains.
* • * *
The old Balmain Steam Ferry Co. is about the
mostoheerfulinstance of a crushed monopoly thatTuß Bul-
letin is aware of. Balmain, a “ working-man’s suburb,”
is one of the most populous in Sydney; and the ferry-
boats to and from the city carry thousands of passengers
daily. Two or three years ago the whole trade was in the
hands of what is now called “the old c 0.,” which
charged a cash fare of 3d. for 7 to 15 min. journey, with
weekly and monthly tickets in proportion. Many appeals
were made for a reduction of rates; but the directors,
strong in monopoly, hardened their hearts and gave a
short and haughty answer to every plea for mercy. Then
the desperate Balmainiacs started a co-operative steam
ferry, called the Balmain New Ferry Co., amid the jeers
of the old oo.’s supporters, who prophesied speedy failure.
But every hard-working artisan who had groaned under
the old co.’s tariff took a share or two in the new
venture, and stuck to it, and persuaded his friends to
stick to it, and often walked a long distance round to it
in order to avoid travelling by the old co.’s boats; and
the old co.’s trade on one route fell away to nothing
instanter. Then it reduced its fares below the Id.
which the new co. charged ; but the workmen stuck
together like wax and were supported by everybody in
Balmain who understood the value of opposition. The
consequence now is that the new co. is making money
handover fist, while the old one is as continuously losing
it, and meets next week to decide whether it will throw
up the sponge altogether. As an example of successful
co-operation, the Balmain New Ferry Co. is worth
watching.
* * » •
A weird and wonderfully circuitous scheme of
bank-amalgamation is proposed in Melbourne. The idea
is to slump together the liquid business and available cash
of five local institutions—the National, Victoria, Colonial,
City of Melbourne, and Commercial of Australia—and
make one vast concern, to be called the Cnnsolidat d Bank
of Victoria. Then the dead business, the unsaleable lands,
unrealisable overdrafts, had debts, and all the miscel-
laneous securities for which there is no market, are to he
lumped together into another concern to be called the
Consolidated Securities Corporation. The bank would
thus have some £13,000,000 in liquid assets and cash, and
would become responsible for £7,000,000 of new current
accounts and other liabilities; while the Corporation
would have all the heavy and unmarketable assets, and
would be responsible for the reconstructed deposits
amounting to i2l 000,000 or more. 'I hen the worst part
of the confusion begins. The Corporation is to hold, for
the time, all the shares of the bank as additional security
for the reconstructed depositors. The preference share-
holders of the existing hanks are to give up their shares
and get instead 5 per cent, shares of the Corporation
—the thing with all the dead assets in it. The
ordinary shareholders of the existing hanks are to give up
their shares and receive instead ordinary shares in the
Corporation ; so that the same old crowd of shareholder
will own the S' curities Corporation, and the Corporation
will own the Consolidated Bank. This being fixed up the
reconstructed depositors come in, and the confusion gets
worse.
The bank, which is owned by the Corporation,
is to issue to the Corporation in part payment of the re-
constructed deposits, £3,000,000 in 3 per cent, consols.
The balance of the “ fixed ” deposits are to be paid, one-
half in “maximum 3 per cent, preferred debentures,”
payable in 25 years, and one-half in “maximum 3£ per
cent, debentures” which are not to be paid at all hut are lo
go on for ever. Further, the reconstructed depositors are
to receive a cash bonus of 6 per cent, as an inducement,
and an attempt is to be made to persuade Govt, to
guarantee the 3 per cent, perpetual debentures. The
result of all this learned and elaborate hash is that the
reconstructed depositor is to get an average of 3Jper cent.


STRAIGHT TIP.
Drink like a fish—water only. Smoke like know-
ing ones—Cavour’s only. This is sound and good
advice. Neither water nor Oavour cigars will ever
hurt you ; both are pure and cost little. Cavour’s
are mild and aromatic. May be had at eight for
a shilling. Get them—the sooner the better.
Delay is a fitter.
Instead of the present 4J ; that he is to have the
dead assets as his security, instead of the whole resources
of the hanks ; that the two reasonably good banks are to
combine on even terms with the three dubious ones ; that
calls are to stop—for the present, anyhow ; and that there
will be a state of frantic confusion in which nobody will
know “ where he are."
Meanwhile, City of Melbourne Bank deposits
have no buyers above 65., and sellers are quite willing to
take Bs., therefore, whatever is to be done in this par-
ticular case looks as if it would need to be done quickly.
If tiie Vic. Government offered 2 per cent, debentures to
the fixed depositors of this institution the chances are, in
the present state of things, that the offer would be cheer-
fully accepted. Then the State could charge the bank
2J per cent., and thus make a handsome profit in the
transaction, and could take over the entire control until
the poor little concern is once more on its feet. Even
the City of Melbourne would get through on such terms
as these, with a State guarantee behind it. There is
a fine chance for a hard and enterprising Scotch financier
to distinguish himself at the Vic. Treasury, just now.
* • • •
Next time, and every other time, that an Aus-
tralian Government issues a local loan it should be
enacted that the dividends thereon shall be subject to an
absentee-tax of 25 per cent. As a solid guarantee against
the debentures drifting into the hands of the inevitable
J. Bull, and helping to take more interest out of the
country, this simple device would be wonderfully effec-
tive. J. Bull could have Che debentures if he liked to buy
them, but instead of £3 10s. per cent, interest he would
only get £2 12s. 6d., which would make all the difference.

The liquidator of the Land Co. of Australasia
reports that one of the mysteries of that truly remark-
able institution was the loss of £B5OO in the notorious
Last Call gold-mine. The amount appeared in the books
first as owing by one of the directors, and then after a
while it was written off among the losses, and disappeared.
Whether the director was speculating in mining shares on
the company’s account, or whether he speculated on his
own account and charged his losses to the 00., isn’t ex-
plained. Anyhow, the shareholders should hunt up this
director, and take a firm hojd of him, and not let go until
the matter is fully explained. Another of the Land Co.’s
troubles was an "estate ” which it bought for £49,000.
It paid £27,000, and £6OOO in interest, and then let the
seller have the property back again, but now the land is
only valued at £7OOO, so the co. owes him another £15,000
and £2OOO for rnore’interest, in addition to losing the pro-
perty. In other words, it-buys a property for £49,000, and
has to pay £50,000 to induce the original owner to take it
back and keep it, and say nothing more about it. Look-
ing at lids fearsome transaction in all its aspects, The
Bulletin is of opinion that some “representative busi-
ness men ” have less brains than an ordinary lunatic. The
dire-tors who could fix up a £49,000 land-purchase so
that it became necessary to pay £50,000 to induce the
previous owner to keep tire properly, are individuals
whose capacity for tying themselves in a hard knot is
something sublime.

* * • «
The Australian Temperance and General In-
surance Society reports that during the past year it has
issued 686 policies, yielding a premium income of about
£4OOO, and has added £22,798 to its funds. The Temper-
ance and General is an office which has existed and
silently petrified for 19 years. It never seemed to get
really afloat, and its whole accumulated funds, after 19
years of toil, would hardly make a year’s income for a
good second-flight society. Nobody seems to know the
cause of this state of tilings, except that the office has
been a poor, slow, little concern all the days of its life,
and has never been properly alive. Still, in its own small
way, judged on its own figures—and in the absence of a
State audit every society has to be judged on its own
figures—it seems to be moderately sound. Its expenses,
apart from its industrial business, are about 21 per cent,
of its entire income—a woefully high figure certainly, but
one that is by no means unprecedented. The item, " out-
standing and accrued interest,” represents about one-
third of the year’s revenue from investments. As it is
impossible to say how much of this is “outstanding ”
and how much is only “ accrued,” the soundness of the
investments is difficult to estimate, but they seem to be
reasonably good. The only serious objection to the Tem-
perance and General is its name and the fact that it is a
burlesque little institution which can hardly be regarded
seriously, and which takes itself very seriously all the
same. If it gave up taking itself seriously, and professed
to be a somewhat humorous institution, it would be a
great success.
The Temperance and General does not, how-
ever, represent the humblest aspect of Australian life
assurance business. A still smaller concern is the Vic-
toria General Life Co., which has an annual premium
income of £6877, just about as much as the A.M.P. society
collects in a day and a half. And right at the bottom of
the list comes the Adelaide Lite Co., which, after 28 years’
of existence, has a premium income of £lO3l per year
about a day’s income for the Colonial Mutual, or as much
as the A.M.P. Society collects in two working hours. This
u dque institution, when last heard of by this paper, had
117 policies in existence ; it spends 69J per cent, of its
premium income on working expenses, and has a total
fund of about £40,000. Probably not one person in a
thousand even knows of the existence of these quaint
little concerns which hang on to the extreme tail-end of
the life insurance industry in Australia. There isa sleepy,
restful, old-cobwebby aspect about them. They don’t
advertise much, or rush about and perspire in the effort
to acquire business, and tbeir dreamy existence is vaguely
suggestive of a hermit mooning in his grotto and letting
all the outer world slide.
* ♦ • *
Actuary Alsop, of Melb. Savings Bank, adversely
criticises the local P.O. Savings Bank. The P.O. Bank
will not take cheques, or any deposit that is not “even
shillings £1 Is. but not £1 Is. 6d. The P.O. Bank also
posts an acknowledgment of every deposit—which inter-
f -res with the privacy of the thing. But actuary Alsop
shouldn’t throw stones. Melb. Savings’ Bank’s system
is cumbrous and unnecessary. The pass-book must accom-
pany every lodgment and withdrawal. Thus, depositor
must do all his bank-work himself or else “give away”
Ibe state of his account to everyone who acts for him.
The popular idea is that these shackles are put on to
prevent tradespeople from using the bank for current
accounts.
Now, the frequent drawing of cheques on a
“savings”bank coni.l be stopped by regulation, if the bank
must stick to the letter of its name. By the irony of fact,
the Bank is now used for trade current accounts-and
persons with “givings” are frequently frightened away
by the ritual. In fact, Melb. Savings Bank is run for the
benefit of the “tlirifty” person who will each week under-
go all sorts of inconvenience for the sake of 7d. “interest”
in the half year. Melb. Savings Bank, viewed in the light
of its mortgages, is unsatisfactory ; and, in the light of
its ritual, is ridiculous. The sooner it makes way for the
State Bank the better.

Dear Bulletin, —Would it not pay to locally
manufacture sterling silver table-requisites, such as
spoons and forks ? The lowest price quoted in Sydney for
these goods is from 7s. to 7s, 6 1. per oz., and with silver
selling at less than 2s. 6d. per oz., there is a big profit
somewhere. The best electroplate can be bought for
about Is. 6d. per oz. taking the above goods at per doz.
As the cost of making a silver spoon should not exceed
the amount which will supply material, manufacture, and
furnish two or more profits, on an electroplate ditto, it
appears as if sterling silver goods could be sold profitably
at about ss. an oz.—Billycan.

Ed. Bulletin,—The Vic. Ministry intends to
amend the Insolvency Act. ’Twere better to introduce
another on these lines: The abolition of all assignments,
liquidation by arrangement, and composition by arrange-
ment, anyone unable to pay 20s. in the £to sequestrate
and become an ordinary insolvent. Abolish trustees,
have only official assignees who shall be assisted in wind-
ing up the estate by a committee of inspection elected at
a general meeting of creditors held seven days after filing
of the schedule ; such committee to receive no remunera-
tion. The assignee shall examine the insolvent within 14
days of filing of schedule, and ns often as 1 e thinks fit; a
report of every examination shall be submitted to the
Judge, who may order further examination by th“
assignee or in open court, and name a lawyer to aid if
necessary. All examinations to be public, and deposi-
tions to be taken in writing. The committee shall
also report to the Judge, and should any friction arise
with the assignee, the Judge shall be arbitrator and may
obtain expert assistance. Commission at 5 per cent, should
be charged on the gross amount realised, per cent, to
the assignee and the rest to the Govt. ; the assignee to be
held personally responsible for the probity of all his em-
ployes. All accounts to be approved and signed by com-
mittee and assignee, also subject to taxation. All credi-
tors to have free access to accounts, and rightof complaint
direct to the Judge. A bill on these lines would exter-
minate the expert and greedy swindlers to be found in the
ranks of the trade assignees, and cause consternation
amongst their satellites, the shady solicitors. Small
creditors could not then be coerced into accepting Id. in
the £ by majorities who are afraid to allow their blunders
to see the light; in fact, it would prevent,the Insolvency
Court being a chapel of ease, as it is at present to the
knowing and unscrupulous.—G.
