From 1493 to 1893, inclusive, the estimated
amount of gold and silver produced in the world was:
Gold, 406,223,949 fine oz. ; silver, 7,663,409,716 fine oz.
This is in the proportion of soz. of gold to 950 z. of silver,
and, strangely enough, this is very like the proportion in
which gold and silver were produced in '9l, '92, and ’93.
The totals for these three years vary verv much, but the
ratio of production is exactly the same "for the three—
namely, 4 - 4 oz. of gold to 95'60z. of silver, or 1 to 2173.
But the commercial ratio (price) has varied considerably :
’9l, 1 to 20-92 ; ’92, 1 to 2372 ; ’93, 1 to 26‘49. 1
Director R. E. Preston, of U.S.A. Mint, issues
a very optimistic estimate of the States’ gold-product for
’94. The ’93 product was 35,955.000d015.—but for the
past year a sum of 42 to 45 millions dollars is expected.
If the larger estimate be realised it will mean no less than
2,175,000 fine ounces—a total before which Australasia
may hide herdiminished head. The same authority quotes
38,000,000d015. for Australasia, which would mean 1,837,000
fine ounces, but recent local statistics do not induce The
Bulletin to desert its own figures, namelv, 2,065 000
ounces gross, or, say, 1,900,000 ounces fine. " ’
The African figures come next in importance.
Gold is produced to a small extent on the West Coast, as
well as to a large extent in the Transvaal. The West
Coast production is reckoned as the amount imported
thence by England, the figures for ’92 being 48 9520 z.
fine. The Randt out-put for Sept, was 176,7070 z., making
the total to that date 1,493,3730 z. The close of 1894
should therefore see a grand total of 2,030,0000 z. (crude),
the value of which is £3 12s. per oz. The balance of the
Transvaal would turn out, say, 120,0000 z. of the same
quality. The ’94 African product may therefore be put
thus in fine ounces:—
Randt 1,720,425
Rest of Transvaal .. .. 101,700
West Coast 50,000
1.872.125
“The world’s output of gold for ’93 was the largest in
history,” so says the U.S.A. Mint. It is now certain that
’94 will eclipse ’93.
Of the 7,523,0000 z. of gold produced in 1893,
4,883,0000 z. came from U.S.A., Africa, and Australasia—
leaving 2,640,000 for the rest of the world to produce.
The latter amount will be much increased for last
year, if only by the mining activity in South America—but
this increase can be left as a discount for optimism in
other directions.
The 1894 estimate for the world can, therefore,
be stated as follows:
U.S.A. .. .. .. 2,175,000
Australasia .. .. 1,900,000
Africa .. ... .. 1,872,125
Other countries .. .. 2,640,000
8.587.125
This view indicates an increase on the world’s previous
record for one year of about 1,064,0000 z., or £4,522,000.
The importance of these statistics to the cur-
rency tangles and theories of the day cannot be exag-
gerated—yet each party will consider them conclusive for
its own end. But let any old miner, who thinks that the
day of gold is past, contemplate the following comparison
of the days of the old rushes with to-day and yesterday :
Average world’s yield .. ’sl—’ss .. 6,410,324
» » .... ’s6—’6o ... 6,486,262
„ „ .. ’66—’7o „ 6,270.086
>. ’Bl—’Bs .. 4,794,755
Total yield .. .. 1889 .. 5,973,780

Estimate .. .. 1894 .. 8,587,125
This means that ’94 will produce much over 900 tons of
pure gold—or, more exactly, the equivalent of 36,500,000
golden sovereigns. This is the biggest century in gold-
mining, and ’94 is the biggest year of the century, so far.

The estimate of 670,5440 z. as the Yic. gold-yield
is based on the figures of the Vic. mining registrars. Yet
the amount of Vic. gold actually coined last year from
Jan. 1 to Dec. 31 was 739,4960 z. Naturally many small
amounts find their way to the banks which the registrars
never hear of.
The 4,166,874 new aovs. minted in Melbourne
last year are equal to a daily dole of 11,500 lor the year,
including Sundays.
During ’94, Sydney melted and re-coined
17,1400 z. of worn sovs. and half sovs.—nearly £67,000.
Melbourne only dealt with about £lOOO in the same way.
The gross value of gold handled by Sydney
Mint since its establishment in ’55 to 31st Dec. last, equals
£79,204,132, of which £3,084,778 was received in ’94. The
new sovs. and half-sovs. issued during the 49 years
totalled £76.072,000. of which less than £11,000,000
remain in N.S. W.
Melb. Mint finished ’94 with a record of
1,061,6230 z.— which easily surpasses the previous largest
turnover, namely, 946,9960 z. for '93. Barely 200,0000 z. of
Westralian gold went to Melb. in ’94.

Jolm E. Leete, in the North American Review:
“ The purchasing-power of gold has doubled during the
life of any debt that is 20 years old, and the lender
receives with his interest twice as much as he lent.”

Tasmanian mining still booms. An Bft. reef,
carrying splendid gold, is reported from Mt. Jukes, West
Coast. The Comet is opening up splendidly, and a huge
ore-body, 22ft. wide, assaying up to 9£oz. gold and 1240 z.
of silver, besides copper, is reported from near Mt. Reid.

* * * *
Gympie’s 1894 yield was 111,5070 z. gold from
85,002 tons stone. Divs. of £229,023 exceeded nominal
calls by £133,333, and the outlook is brilliant. The area
of operations is extending, and the number of paying
claims seems on the increase. Last year is the best since
1884.
• * * •
Victorian coal output for 1894 was 176,918 tons.
This almost doubles.that for 1893, 91,726 tons; and is
nearly eight times th* total for 1892, 23,363 tons. Which
rapid increase of production is accompanied by a steady
decrease in the quantity imported from N.S. Wales,
—in ’9l, 954,277 tons ; in ’92, 879,068 tons; and in '93,
731,304 tons. In these figures the Newcastle colliers may
read one effective cause of slack employment.
• * ♦ •
Wyalong total yield to date is given as 40840 z,
from 2615 tons. This is a good average ; but when you
remember it represents, say, 3000 men’s work for 12
months—which should be a low estimate, considering
that at one time there were said to be 10,000 on the
ground—there isn’t much in it. It pans out a div. of
£5 per man per annum. Call it 1500 men for six months,
and there’s only £2O per man per annum—not “ten bob a
week.” A fellow could make more than that out of the
Labor Bureau.
* » • *
The cutting of the Victory reef at 1090 ft. in the
Bonnie Dundee shaft, Charters Towers, is a big thing
for the field —and for the English shareholders, who, how-
ever, have deserved luck by pluck. The reef is 2ift.
thick, and the stone is estimated to go 2oz. A lot of
country in the direction of the Queen Central and
Brilliant Central is now proved. Heavy buying orders
were cabled from London when the news was known, and
recent stock exchange biz. has been phenomenal.
* * * *
Hillgrove (N.S.W) has just completed a year of
hard work and fair profit. Baker’s Creek, the leading
claim, crushed 10,928 tons for 17.3980 z., and paid £30,000
in divs. The Eleanora, which relies almost as much on
antimony as on gold, crushed 9229 tons for 33940 z., and
produced 350 tons concentrates ; also, 471 tons antimony
and 24 tons white metal—valued in all £24,748. Sunlight's
yield was 47640 z. from 11,696 tons ; and the stone is ap-
parently improving, last two crushings going over the
crucial Aoz.

♦ * * *
Another call on the Kapai-Vermont shares—
another proof that between the estimated and real cost of
mining plants there is usually a big distinction, sometimes
attributable to ignorance and sometimes to deception.
Old Bendigo looks as if it might have a new
spurt in ’95. Last year showed a dreadful falling off, the
divs. (£125,000) being only half those of '93, and rather
less than half those of ’92. Within the last few weeks,
however, Lansell’s “180” mine has opened up a
reef at 3100 ft., and a crosscut at a depth of
900 ft. has discovered a promising lode lying
parallel with the great New Chum line, and some-
thing over 16ft. thick. If this pans out well, it ought to
give a new lease of life all along the New Chum line. The
New Chum Railway mine has also got a big and promising
formation at 2800 ft., and these discoveries look as if there
was another great future in front of Bendigo. The belief
that the reefs go right down to perdition or thereabouts
is beginning to revive, and the discovery of a forgotten
lode, only 400 ft. away from ihe New Chum line, makes
some sanguine spirits imagine that the gold deposits,
even in the upper regions, are only scratched after all. In
1877, 1879, and again in 1889, Bendigo suffered an even
worse drop than that of 1894, but in each case it had a
glorious resurrection and started afresh, and no one can
imagine how many more resurrections may lie before it.

A. J. Wilson, of the Investor’s Review, dis-
charges some of his most hair-raising Billingsgate at the
crowd of Westralian boomers in London. If these mining
potentates don’t proceed against him on a large scale for
libel damages, the only possible inference is that neither
their their doings can bear public inspection.
A certain syndicate had an expert out in search
of any show good enough to float a co. on. Having come
across a good claim in the hands of five miners, he
arranged for the purchase of the mine for so many paid-
up shares only, in a 00. to be floated to work it. Flota-
tion was effected, and the directorate consisted of the
syndicate, who bought machinery and got the co. into
debt. The batik, which had granted an overdraft on the
personal guarantee of the directors, foreclosed—the move
being for one of the syndicate to buy the claim in privately
and get rid ol the paid-up shareholders, viz., the vendor*.
The vendors saw the move and carried on a long law-
suit, only to lost', as they had no money. However, while
they were fighting other troubles arose, and the claim
finally (jot out of both parties’ reach. The miners went
prospecting once more, and when asked their opinion of
Sto k Exchange gentlemen their language invariably
•tarts a bush-fire.

The Mitchell Creek freehold mine, N.S.W.,
started its chlorination works the other day with an ex-
traction of 93 per cent. With another 10-head the output
should be almost doubled, and the mine can only pay
substantial divs. on a big output.

along the line of Menzies’ find, about 120 miles N. of
Coolgardie, and 30 to 40 miles from the Ninety-Mile. This
is about the best show of payable milling ore in a fairly
large body of stone on the Coolgardie field, though the
writer has heard it whispered that the gold is not so good
at 50ft.

Bayley’s Reward is rapidly running down-hill.
Last week’s output, “120 tons for 400 oz.” What price
Bayley’s shares six months hence? people are asking.
Already down to 12s.—a fall of 50 per cent, in three
months ! Yet- there is a lot of rich stone stacked below,
and when she has fallen to zero she may arise once more,
and knock on the jaw-point the people who are shorten-
ing the script.
• • • *
Everything still is quiet as a graveyard about
the recent alleged find at Bayley’s No. 1 South, where
IOOOoz. were picked out in two hours. Some are in the
“know”—hence recent anxious inquiries for shares,
which have been increased three for one. Present price,
16s. Under these cheerful circumstances it is easy to pre-
dict the crushing will be a good one. But let not the
public accept that first crushing as typical.

and 1000 feet will average Bft. thick. Moreover, it will
crush ounces, not weights."

At Cue, Murchison, there are several short
table-top hills. The quartz-reefs occur as horizontal
beds from a few inches to 3ft. thick, coursing through the
country in all directions, but with a slight incline to the
centre of the hills. The quartz is of milky whilenessand
carries, perhaps, 2oz. per ton, but as the quantity is
limited it is of no great value to any company.
On the edge of this belt there are a number of
small reefs running north and south, with smaller reefs
running at right angles to the main lodes. The molt
promising claims on this line are the Lady Mary,
Cue I, and the Rising Sun. These have been developed
to a depth of 200 ft., showing a fair but very erratic reef, as
the quartz is in and out every few feet and varies from
a small seam to 3ft. They mill from 2oz. to 3oz. per ton.
On the outer edge of the belt is the Lily line of
reef, the quartz of which is not so high grade. It is
being developed hy Streeter preparatory to floating in
London.
The next line of reef is the Leviathan, on which
are the Kangaroo Gem and other mines. The quartz is
an altered granite marked hy large blows on the surface
which form small reefs below from a few inches to 2ft’
wide. The quartz is frozen to the wall in places, and
divided into several small stringers mixed with the
country rock.
To the west of the Leviathan line are the reefs
of the Dead Finish district, of which there are a great
number. They might be described as specimen reefs, too
poor to mill in quantity.
South from Dead Finish and west of Cue are
the reefs of the Day Dawn district, of which the Day
Dawn is the most developed. The refcf outcrops from 6
to 18ft. wide, dipping on an incline of 65deg. west. It is
well-equipped with a 20-stamp mill and hoisting-gear.
One ore-shoot 160 ft. long has been opened up, and, so far
has milled an average of 15dwts. For some time the
tailings assayed Sdwts , but under Mr. Armstrong’s
management the mill is doing better work, leaving only
4dwts. in the tailings, and this amount, no doubt, will be
reduced shortly.
The Trenton is west of the Day Dawn about
half-a-mile, and owned by the Day Dawn Association. The
reef is short, showing a slight outcrop for a few hundred
feet, and the mines north and south have so far failed to
find it.
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Unole Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peaoh and ivory may be had by anyone who make#
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

Personal Items.
Gov. William Robinson, Westralia, will be
61 years old on Monday, Jan. 14.
Michael Davitt is due next month at Mel-
bourne, where he starts his lecturing tour.
Secretary Wallington, in Melbourne as in
Sydney, has earned the title of Governor’s taoto-
tum.
The very latest is that N.S.W. Premier
Reid “left Caledonia 60 days after he was born.”
This on his own authority.
Father Robinson, a Walcha (N.S.W.) R.C.
priest, has invented a gold-saving machine. The
Bulletin presumes it is shaped like a collection-
plate.
The Age regrets that Tom Bent did not
receive a title. So will every good Australian.
Bent would be an excellent companion of Michael
and George.

Mr. ROBERT ETHERIDGE, Jun., the new
Curator of the Australian Museum, Sydney.
A very prominent Sydney teetotaller no
longer sports his blue ribbon at church since the
parson refused to patronise unfermented sacra-
mental wine.
One night in Yic. Assembly, auctioneer
Patterson was expressing his disgust at “big,
strong men pumping up pints of beer behind a bar
counter,” “It’s not worse,” replied MTntyre,
“ than a gigantic man, with a little hammer,
knocking down nothing at all !”
The high-kicking Girl of Yar-
mouth is a raw youth whose experi-
ences of life have hitherto been
limited to Eton schooldays, cram-
ming at a tutor’s for the army,
county yeomanry parades, and the
haw-haw society of West End
London and rustic ancestral halls.
Some day he will be a bloated earth-
owner of 70,000 acres, yielding the
comfortable annual revenue of
<£oo,ooo. This small strip of land
is spread over Warwickshire, County
Down, and County Antrim, and
was mainly derived from the Con-
way family. Most of the Yarmouth
family wealth came from a bequest
by the last Duke of Queensberry
(“oldQ.”)to his natural daughter,
wife of the third marquess.
Two ladies and two children
were, on a recent day, waiting at a
South African railway-station, but
their train was not expected for some
hours. Cecil Rhodes, the local
Julius Caesar, came along, and a
“ special ” with two coal-trucks was
provided for his Masterfulness,
The ladies asked leave of the station-
master to travel per “special.” The
official referred them to Rhodes.
Said Csesar : “ AH' right; put ’em
in a coal-truck.” And then the
Dictator entered the only saloon
carriage. Which recalls that the
late Joshua Peter Bell, Acting-
Governor of Queensland, more than
once gave up his “special” carriage
to ladies who had invaded it before-
hand, and retired himself to a com-
mon second-class. It was a favorite
trick of women who knew Bell’s
courtesy to their gender.
Postmaster - General Duffy
(Vic.), when he applied to be admit-
ted as a solicitor, was met by the
same obstacle which confronted Ash,
M.P., of Adelaide. The rules pro-
vide that during the terms of his
articles the clerk must follow no t
business or occupation for profit,
and Duffy, as a paid M.L.A., was
objected to. The Vic. Supreme
Court, however, held that Duffy
didn’t follow politics for pay, but
through love of his bleeding country,
and he pulled through.
The fly in Chief Justice Griffith’s ointment
is that, in the event of a gubernatorial interregnum,
he cannot, as Chief Justices Madden and Darley
can, assume vice-regal airs. Old Arthur Palmer
snatched that “ distinction ” some time ago.
The proprietor of Tit - Bits has been
created a baronet —probably because he printed an
article re Queen Victoria’s dolls. It will not take
long for the Newnes to wear off and to make the
thief-paper’s boss part of “our old nobility.”
The oldest Judge on the Australian Bench
is Mr. Forbes, of N.S.W. District Court (son of
the first N.S. Wales Chief Justice), who will be
76 on May 19ch.
Apropos the Eureka flag. What became of
M’Douall Stuart’s? He carried it from Adelaide,
and unfurled it at Carpentaria. It was made by
a Miss Chalmers.
Anent T. S Mort’s prophecy that G. H.
Reid would become N.S.W. Premier : Has any

S.A. Assembly, was born Dec. 19, 1833. A man

must have much grit.

impounded as security for a night’s revelry by a

Sam Griffith, G.C.M.G. Another step
towards oblivion. Notable that as a man goes up
in the estimation of “ his Queen ” he should go
down in that of his country.

Queenslander lately defunct, left a book of early
Australian reminiscences in MS., which G. B.
Barton is revising for the press.

reminder of Gov. Kintore’s glorious reign will be
Sammy Way’s Pomeranian pup is sad evidence of
the emptiness of worldly things.

appointment to N.S.W. Supreme Court, Justices
Owen and Manning being the only two, out of
seven, who never entered politics.

(Cardinal Archbishop) and Melbourne are Irish-

Bishop Anderson, the newly-elected of
Riverina, spent several years at curate-work in
the Flinders district, N.Q., where the shearers

of salvation.
Premier Turner is reported to own the
most undecipherable handwriting in Victoria. He
is also the most illegible statesman in Australia.
Compositors, as a rule, demand extra pay for
setting up Turner’s MS.
Mr. Nicholas Fitzgerald, M.L.C., who has
just returned to Castlemaine, after a trip to
Europe, and ex-Premier Patterson, of Victoria,
agree that in all their travels they have found no
climate like that of Castlemaine. This probably
means no climate which suits them so well. One
can easily believe such to be the case with Mr. J.
B. Patterson. i

A Sydney lawyer, E. P. Field, is at the
head of the local society for converting the Jews
to Christianity. A professional lawyer laboring
to save the soul of a professional Jew is, as a sug-
gestive spectacle, more fin-de-siecle than the New
Woman.
Stoddart, captain of the English Eleven,
is a London stock-broker; and Phillipson, who is
also independent, follows no occupation. Ford is
a private tutor; M'Laren has a post in the office
of a Manchester manufacturer ; while Gay teaches
the young idea how to shoot at Brighton.

Exhibition delegates, they waited on a N.S.W.
potentate to obtain his official co-operation in the
Hobart, Exhibition scheme. Their account of the
interview is most graphic. “We went to Sydney
and told him that as little Tasmania had helped
the N.S.W. Exhibition long ago, the compliment
should be returned. He jumped down our throats

Straits Times distinguishes between the
attitude toward the public of Cecil Smith, the late
Governor of Straits -Settlement, and the present
Mitchell : “ Sir Cecil wore the sun-helmet of the
Orient—or a felt hat. Sir Charles and his staff
wear the high hat of European civilisation.”
The Age, last week, spoke of the Vic.
Chief-Justice as “Sir John M‘Madden.” This
was cruel to the man (successor to “George Higin-
botham, Chief-Justice ”) who insists upon all
writs being issued in the name of “His Honor the
Honorable Sir John Madden, Knight, LL.D.”
Mrs. Ivo Bligh, wife of the English
amateur cricketer, is on a visit to Melbourne. It
is ten years since the handsome B-echworth girl
married the heir-presumptive of the Earl of Darn-
ley. Mrs. Bligh is a tall brunette who, unlike
the ordinary peeress, really meets the popular
idea of an aristocrat.
A most disgraceful scandal has just been
hushed up. It concerns an individual who, having
left Europe under unspeakable circumstances, was
endowed with an official position in Australia. He
has apparently been at his old game again, and a
warrant issued for his arrest by an independent
magistrate will probably not be allowed to take
effect.
It is not correct, as stated by Mayor Lees,
that George Thornton, still in the flesh, was the
first Mayor of Sydney. Mr. Thornton was Mayor
in 1857, and was the first after the city had
been administered by Commissioners ; but John
Hosking, merchant, of the old-time firm of
Hughes and Hosking, was first Mayor of Sydney
in 1842.
John Raven, who lately died at Hobart,
aged 72, was the son of an Anglican clergyman,
and was born in Tasmania, He had in turn been
sailor, constable, sergeant of detectives in N.S.W.,
miner, clerk, pressman ; and one of the acts of his
declining years was to send to a large London
publishing house a big box of MS. poems, essays,
tales, plays and operas, upon which many years
had been spent. The box was returned only a
few weeks ago after an absence of two years—
unopened. Then John Raven died.
Parkes’ boast at Mayor Lees’ banquet
that while Premier he never asked Mr. Black,
Mr. Brown, or Mr. Grey to vote for a Govern-
ment measure, recalls an ex - Judge Foster
story. Foster, then N.S.W. Attorney-General,
had been denied preferment to the Bench,
and in his wrath exclaimed, “And I supported
the measures you brought in!” “You did,”
replied Parkes. “ And this is my reward !” cried
the incensed Bench-hunter. “If you thought the
measures good, your reward lies in the conscious-
ness of your having supported them,” said Parkes,
“and if they were bad yon should have retired
from the Government rather than be a party to
their passing.” Then the political moralists
looked hard at each other and refused to shake
hands the next time they met.
Rev. John Turner died in Melb., the other
day. In 1850, Government gave him a valuable
allotment, corner of Lonsdale and Stephen streets.
He built there the Particular Baptist Church, not
much bigger than a large saloon railway-carriage.
It remains; and is said to contain all the saved of
Melbourne who are not already food for Particular
Baptist worms.
The National Scripture League (Melb.)
waited on R.C. Archbishop Carr, the other day,
to get a wrinkle about the management of religious
schools. Times are changed since ’4B, when
Father Geoghegan, the first Melb. priest, sent up
his card to Protestant Bishop Perry on the latter’s
arrival, and Dr. Perry refused to see the minister
of a “Satanic delusion.”
Of the late gilded Bobbie
Rand’s close-fistedness, this story is
told. A married manager on one of
bis stations had strict orders never
to buy potatoes whan they were over
Bs. a cwt. “Up to 85.,” said Bob-
bie; “if you pay more for them, I
won’t pay for <tliem, d’you under-
stand?” One day Bobbie called at
the station, and dined there, and
though potatoes were then dear,
some fine ones were placed on the
table. “ Those are good spuds,”
said Bobbie. “Yes, very fine,”
murmured the manager. “ But
they cost more than Bs, ?” “Oh,
yes; Bs. lOd.” “Then you can’t
book ’em to the station. I won’t
pay-, “ These aren’t station pota-
toes. My wife is very fond of pota-
toes, and I paid for these out of my
own pocket.” “ Oh, that’s all right,
so long as it’s understood that I
don’t pay for them.” . . . When
Bobbie was leaving ; “Oh, I say ;
you might put half-a-hundred of
those potatoes in my buggy for me.
I’ll take’em.” “ All right.” Mana-
ger shortly appears with “ half-a-
hundred ” tied up in a bag, an 1 two
fine potatoes visible in his hand.
Money counted out —4s. exactly.
Bobbie drives off. “ Coo-ee !’’
Stops. “Hold on a bit.” Mana-
ger walks leisurely to buggy, hold-
ing out the two fine potatoes afore-
said. “Thqse are fine potatoes,
Mr. Rand?” “ Yes, very fine ; is
that all you want to say?” “Oh,
no ” —putting the potatoes in his
pcoket—“ I noticed you enjoyed
’em yesterday at dinner, and I
thought you’d like to say good-
bye to them.” “You forget that
I have some in the bag he a’
“Oh, them! Them’s station j ota-
toes, and only cost 7s, 3d. a cwt.
Good day.” Bobbie pulled awry
face, cursed a bit, but drove off
with the spuds.
Bishop Green, of Grafton and Armidale,
N.S.W., the still raw victim of platitudinous con-
gratulations, discovers suddenly that one-half of
the fund which should support him has been
lost in unremunerative speculations. The Bishop
thought that those who appointed him to
the See preferred him to English candidates for
“ his pluck and ability,” but he is able to see now
that it was because he was “ green,”
Mrs. Ballance is going to erect a splendid
monument over the grave of her late husband,
and, by the irony of things, the dead statesman V
memorial-stone is being prepared in Hingland,
and is to be shipped out to Maoriland in a week
or two. Think of Ballance, the Protectionist,
whose whole policy was devoted to making Maori-
land self-supporting in every possible respect,
sleeping under an imported monolith from the
land of sweating and unlimited competition !
Truly no man knows what he or his bones may
not come to in the long run.
Mr. John Smith, M.L.O. (N.S.W.), who
died last week, aged 84, arrived from England in
1836, and became manager of a western station,
which he afterwards bought. As far back as 1842
he purchased the Gamboola run, Molong, and
steadily acquired station property until he became
one of the largest western land-owners. No pro-
perty was too small or too large for his purse—he
grabbed the quarter-acre township allotment as
readily as the country carrying three sheep to the
acre ; but he was a land-buyer only, and was
scarcely ever known to sell. He married, in 1841,
the eldest daughter of an old western identity,
“ Parson Torn,” of Guyong, whose name is joined
with that of Hargreaves in the first discovery of
gold at Ophir. A nong Mr. Smith’s children is
the popular wife of Mr. Justice Innes, of N.S.W.
Supreme Court.
G. A. Syme, elder brother of the late
E ienezer and present David, of the Age, is dead.
He began as a preacher, like the other Melbourne
veteran journalist, David Blair, and enjoyed con-
siderable popularity in England. He was thought
to have the best of it in discussions with Holyoake;
but seemed to get much loosened in his own mind,
and thought more freely the older he grew. Long
he dabbled over the T evder, but was never in
his element—a dreamy philosopher, a book in
breeches, rather than a journalist. One odd mis-
take of his, made as a new-ohum, is historical. At
St. Kilda police-station he fished out some deposi-
tions to a murder and published them at length.
They were six months old. Melb. Argus, by-the-
by, utterly ignored G. A. Syme’s death. The
Argus never heard of the Age in all its life.

POLITICALPOINTS
An Imperial knighthood is the fig-leaf of
Australian political indecency.
Tell us—why did the last Yic. general
election knock out all the preachers ?
The whole genius of Government by Con-
tradiction—Don’t be precipitate, but count noses.
“Government by contradiction” is a
casting-out of devils by Beelzebub, prince of the
devils.
The resignation of “ Tom ” Slattery makes
one lawyer less in N.S.W. Assembly. There are
only 16 now.
Mary Lee, boss of the S.A. political
women, says she will never even become a candi-
date for Parliament.
Every N.S.W. Premier since the estab-
lishment of the bicameral system has, at some
stage, threatened to smash the Upper House.
The fact that nearly all the Australian
Parliaments are at loggerheads with the various
Leg. Councils of the country is a healthy sign.
“ The greatest thorn in the side of labor
is the fact that capital has always the means of
introducing alien labor.”— Pastoramsts’ Review.
Then “ get up and bar the door, oh !”
Parson Jones, of Chalmers Church, Melb.,
lately preached from the scriptural indictment of
absentees, contained in Hosea : “Strangers have
devoured his substance.” We are coming on,
N.S.W. Attorney-General Want brought
in a bill, not long ago, for abolition of payment of
members, and now takes two months’ holiday at
.£3O a week, without even a beggarrnan’s blush.
After all the Foreign-trade wails about the
utter evanishment of shipping from the Melbourne
wharves, it is somewhat surprising to find that
the Melbourne shipping for the last few months of
the year was just about the same as that of
Sydney.
In the absence of a satisfactory explana-
tion of the delay, it is discreditable that the
Frenchman, Grenon, under sentence of death at
Sydney for shooting a watchman at Rushcutters’
Bay, should be kept so long in doubt as to his
final fate.

George Black, M.P.

school stood, the other day, with all his weight on
the heels of his boots, and, addressing ex-Premier
Parkes, said : “ The first President of federated
Australia must be an untitled native; if not, then
count me against Federation.”

Government was reduced to such desperate straits

on grocers and butchers. Think of the Australian
ireasury dude getting his salary in tickets entitl-
ing him to steak and potatoes ! Yet it may come
to that.

bdl should introduce it into both houses.
“The late Chief Justice Higinbotham
would have been neither more nor less regarded by
his fellow-citizens if a title had been conferred
upon him,” says the Age. Higinbotham thought
differently, and preserved his good name. The
people would have refused to recognise the noble
George Higinbotham behind an Imperial alias.
Varney Parkes, M.P. (N.S.W.), intends
to reform the system on which estimates of costs
for public works are departmentally made. Some
day aiordly public officer will find his schedule
ot figures, representing the probable cost of,
say, the North Shore Bridge, forwarded by a
Minister to some private firm “for purposes of
verification and check.”
That the N.S. W. Civil Service Commission
will prove a rank and expensive delusion, is, to
anyone acquainted with the work to be done, per-
fectly certain. _ The idea of directing it to bring
u |> a report within three months is a very thin
disguise, as its secretary is appointed at an annual
salary of <£4oo. The work cannot possibly be
done efficiently within even a year.
A similarity has been found between
N.S.W. Premier Reid and Premier Rosebery.
Rosebery is pledged to Horne Rule, but delays it
and would rather go to the country with a cry for
a reconstruction of the House of Lords; Reid is
similarly pledged to Preetrade but delays it, and
is on the qui-vive for a pretext to go to the
country with a cry for the reconstruction of the
Leg. Council.

Every adult male has one vote, and thus the one-man-
one-vote principle is recognised, but, on the other hand,
a double vote is given to married men of 35 years of age,
with a small property qualification, while three votes are
given to members of the learned professions.
“Thus the one-man-one-vote principle is recog-
nised !” So the principle of honesty is “recog-
nised ” by the man who steals his neighbour’s
fowls.
In dealing with the N.S.W. grain-duties,
Premier Reid consistently adhered to his one
seeming ideal of statesmanship—compromise. He
always “splits the difference.” Thus: Closing
one eye, and glassing the other—“ Ahem! duties
now on; my backers want ’em off in 6 months ;
others who may become my backers want ’em left
on for 12 ; halve the difference—leave ’em on till
Sept. 30th, or nine months. Amen. So be it.
(Signed) George Reid.”
Shiels, M.P., backing up a Woman’s
Rights deputation in Melbourne, the other day,
said of the female franchise :
They knew that one province already had this measure*
and he thought it only proper that the name colony of
the Queen, who was the greatest living example of’
woman’s capacity, should adopt it.
“Thegreatest living exampleof woman’s capacity.’
Was there ever a more eloquent argument in
opposition to female suffrage ?
Many of the “faked-up” petitions to Yic.
Legislative Council against the land-tax were
informal according to rule (being printed instead
of written), but were tabled nevertheless. There
were 41 petitions with a grand total of 2922 signa-
tures. Nineteen of them were copies of each other
and eight of these were printed, with blanks left
for the names of localities—obviously issued by the
Reform Club, the Ass, or some kindred body.
.Seven of the technically-correct documents were
from land-banks and mortgage co.’s. This is
Pat-man’s patent “ public opinion.”

A RATIONAL EXCUSE.
"Certificates of previous good character were
handed to the Judge, but he remarked that this
rather told against the prisoner than otherwise,”
&c.. &c.—DAILY PAPER.
THE JUDGE: “ Have you anything to say before
I pass sentence on you? ”
PRISONER: “ Nothing, your Honor—only that I
have borne a bad character all my life,
and I hope you will let me down lightly on
that account .”
The cable announcing Hume Black’s
return to Queensland mentions that he intends
to re-enter political life. No doubt, but Banana-
landers should have something to say on this
subject. Hume Black played all he was worth for
the Agent-Generalship; and when he couldn’t
get it, was proud and happy to become
a joint in MTlwraith’s tail with an Eng-
lish lectureship fee of .£lOOO a year—letting
Separation and bis constituents go hang.
His windy clamor about Queensland resources
was intended to hook immigrants on the one
side and inspire confidence among capitalistic rail-
way syndicators on the other. Nothing has
come of either spec., and as Garrick is gummed to
the Agent-Generalship for 12 months, and as long
after as he can secure, the itinerating Black will
re-enter political life if Queensland voters will let
him. That is, he hopes to go through Parliament
to the same old Agent-Generalship.

When the gilded Captain Pursey Chirn-
side (now suffering from a bad attack of colored
waistcoat) was standing for Grant at Vic. general
election, several electors asked him whether he was
in favor of One-man-one-vote. “Yes,” he re-
plied. “Why did you say that?” his com-
mittee asked him afterwards, “ You aren’t in
favor of it, you know.” “Oh !” said Pursey.
“ Why am I not ? Don’t you think every man
ought to have a vote?” And that was all the
deah boy knew about the subject.
Bendigo, Vic., Presbytery hopes and prays
that all Church properties not actually used for
religious purposes will be exempt from taxation
under the land-tax. Treasurer Turner replies that
the corner pub. and the pawnshop that pay rent
to the “Loord” will have to stump up, and the
Church is petitioning the Legislative Council to
“ make provision for the exemption of all Church
properties.” The Church is prepared to render
unto Caesar the things that are Caesar’s. But it
claims the privilege of defining Caesar’s belongings.
The Roman Quintus Fabius Labeo, being
appointed arbiter to settle a boundary between
the peoples of Nola and Naples, counselled each
separately to do nothing covetously, and rather
to draw back than advance. Both taking this
advice, an unoccupied space was left between
them. He then adjudged to each people the
boundary to which it had confined itself, and all
that was in the middle to the people of Rome.
Here is a suggestion for the settlement of the
Victoria y. S. A. boundary dispute. Let Premier
Reid arbitrate.
The one parson in N.S.W. Assembly—
Rev. G. W. Smailes —has proved a hopeless
cypher in the House. Experience has shown the
parson in politics to be a blank and dismal failure.
The average minister may have some little in-
fluence at mothers’ meetings, and with milk-and-
water young women of both sexes ; but he cannot
rub shoulders with men. The parson is a success
only so long as he talks from the pulpit to an
audience muzzled by the Act of Parliament which
makes it an indictable offence to boo-hoo at or
contradict him.
“ Tom” Slattery, the bogey-man of N.S.W.
politics, who was credited by the daily press with
being at the bottom of every piece of smart
manoeuvring, has retired to look after his law-
business. Thomas was one of the “ properties ”
of the daily papers, and is now spoken of by them
with appropriate tenderness. Had the good-
natured and easy-going Mr. Slattery stuck
to his business as an attorney he could have
been a very rich man. Now, after al4 years’
feast of politics, he goes back to hard work.
Moral : Always do your work before dinner.
Likewise, lay to your book.
Anarchist Andrews has stated that his
defence when the seditious libel prosecution comes
on will* consist in an attempt to prove that he
wrote and spoke in substantially the same words
as N.S.W. Chief Justice Darley used while
sentencing certain fractious shearers. On 4th
Jan. the S.M. Herald in a leading article actually
anticipate* Andrews’ plea, and by emphasising
t'm Judge’s qualifications argues that the meaning
of the judicial utterance is perfectly clear. The
Herald may not have had Andrews in its mind
at the time, but all the same the article is in effect
a Crown Prosecutor’s address to the jury who
will try him. Undoubtedly Andrews will be
justified in challenging every juror who admits to
having read the Morning Herald of the date
mentioned.
At last Yic. general election the public
service vote was given to Labor and Liberalism.
r l he average man, when his pocket is attacked,
will for its defence ally himself, if need be, with
the Devil. This was the public servant’s view of
the situation when he voted for his erstwhile
bogey, the Trades Hall candidate. And now that
he has got back, largely through the aid of the
Labor members, the amounts which J. Beef
Patterson illegally deducted from his salary, and
the reign of terror in the service is over, the high-
collared one will probably abhor the Trades Hall
candidate as heretofore, and vote anew the genteel
Conservative ticket.

The £‘216,000 which N.S.W. Premier
Reid proposes to spend on roads and bridges in
1895 and to debit to the Dibbs budget of 1893,
will make another unnoticed addition to the public
debt of N.S.W. There is no such sum on hand ;
so the money, of course, comes out of loans. G. H.
Reid thus does a great part of the ordinary road
and bridge work of ’95 with borrowed money,
instead of providing for it out of current revenue,
and gets over the difficulty by calling it a deficit
left by his predecessor. According to law the
vote lapsed at the end of 1894, and so the money
can’t be legally spent this year, but it will be
spent illegally all the same.

In his spee'ch at Sydney Caledonian gather-
ing, Premier Reid, of the wondrous frock-coat,
spoke of his fellow-countrymen as “Scotchmen.”
This is most objectionable. There is a wide
difference between “Scot” and “Scotch.” The
Scotchman is the pious stage-fraud with hairy
legs and half-crown-sized freckles—the canting
church-elder who sands the sugar and “ jews”
feur-year-old lolly-buyers; but the Scotsman is
the open-handed, Macdonald-Oameron, sword-
dancing kind of warrior with the 50-inch chest,
and a retinue of six pipers, and a private dis-
tillery in his native glen. To be a Scotsman is
a noble thing, but anyone convicted of being
“Scotch ” deserves seven years. Georgey-Porgey
Reid should paste this in his hat.
* * *
Gov. Duff has made his peace with Sydney
Highland Society, and cemented the bond with
Reid treacle. The vice-president, who last year
suggested that the absent impotentate’s health
should be “drunk in silence,” was prevented from
being a skeleton at this year’s feast by an oppor-
tune journey down the coast, where he stayed
“ weather-bound ” till the danger was all over.
• • *
The claimant of the estate of old “ Bobby ”
Rand, who died intestate, leaving £250,000 behind
him, seems to have come to grief at a very early
stage of his career. His claim, according to the
daily-paper rep irts, is based on tire assertion that
Rand was married before be left England, that
he had a son, and that the man who wants the
money is that son. The case on the other side
is that Rand left England at the age of 12 and
never revisited it, and on the strength of this fact
his legal.representatives desire to put it on record
as their fixed opinion that he must have married
very young. At present the case stands there.
# # •
Gov. Kintore was not too popular in
Adelaide Anglican circles, because he rarely wor-
shipped at the cathedral, but preferred a little
suburban church where an English friend was
curate. Previous Governors especially W.
Robinson— made it fashionable to visit the cathe-
dral and ostentatiously plant a half-sov. in the bag,
and Gov. K., by abolishing this custom, deprived
St. Peter’s Cathedral of hundreds of pounds per
annum.

Some Sydney suburban tram-guards pray
daily for the soul of the tin-pot legislator who
continually parades his trumpery importance at
their expense. Verbatim example. “ Ticket,
please!” “D’you mean to say you don’t know
me?” “No, sir!” Furiously—“ Send the other
guard here ! ” Quietly—” It’s my duty to take
your ticket, sir!” “There, then” fiercely
thrusting out gold “pass”—“and remember, my
man, I’ve taken your number ! ”

remit of drink-worship.

lainy, .he does it “in

“What has Mr. MTubyre done that he
hould be singled out from his fellow-citizens as a
proper subject for distinction by the. Crown?”
asks Melb. Age. Well, he headed a great Sand-
hurst grovel when the Duke of Edinburgh visited
the town. Moreover, the Argus certifies that
MTutyre has done “yeoman service for the con-
stitutional cause in company with Mr. Downes
Carter and others.” Certainly, it is a long time
since Mao. slavered the Duke and assisted the
g'Jted Carter to hold up the Constitution, but the
Crown has a long memory for services of that sort.
And wasn’t he VillageSettlementer in the glorious
Patterson Ministry, which invited the Yorks to
come out and make the country cheerful? The
Bulletin is not surprised at MTntyre’s knight-
hood. The odd thing is that Carter didn’t also
get a “distinction” on account of his late
lamented Budget. What has Carter done that he
should be allowed to remain an ordinary wine-
merchant?
« • •
An Anglican parson, a sincere and able
man, with a wife and eight children, in the diocese
of Ballarat, was engaged at a £250-a-year stipend
which has now tapered down to £l3O paid
grudgingly and at erratic periods. His parish,
200 miles from the seaboard, is, perhaps, the
hottest, dreariest and dearest hole in the province,
and from motives of economy he decided to send
his family to live in Melbourne. This, however,
was angrily vetoed by the congregation, who
objected to their “ thrippences ” going to enrich
the traders of the metropolis. Gloomy fact!
writes a Bulletin correspondent.
* * *
A sign of the times. The chief Church of
England at Broken Hill (N.S.W.) has been sold
by auction to pay a bank overdraft. And all over
Australia ministers are deserting their pulpits
because congregations absolutely refuse to cash
up the subsistence minimum. Australia for the
Heathen !
• * *
It is said that several of the reputed
aborigines in La Perouse camp, near Sydney, are
ex-newspaper editors from the country press. The
story seems feasible, for a N.S.W. £3-a-week
country editorship, advertised the other day,
elicited over 200 applications, including many
from really good men. So much for journalism as
a profession.
* * •
It is suggested in a Sydney paper that
sermons should be abolished. The point is at
least arguable. Washington Irving said that a
man could earn a reputation for wisdom in either
of two ways—by talking a great deal and thinking
very little, or by shutting his mouth and not
thinking at all. Perhaps it’s just about time for
our spiritual advisers to try the latter alternative.
• » *
Apropos a lurid current scandal, it need
only be observed that you can’t expect professional
soldiers to be very moral. They never are. For one
lobster-coated Puritan you can find ten-thousand
rakes. Given well-paid, more or less, idle men
who gain their positions by means in which brain-
power is the least and social influence' the greatest
factor—given, in addition, a set of idle women
who adore brass buttons and Guvmentouse and
glory in general, and what can you expect? No
wonder the padded man usually gets into trouble
in the long run.

Most of the funeral-notices now consist of
about eight lines containing the necessary
announcement, and several lines very unnecessarily
giving the various addresses and telephone-num-
bers of the undertaker. Certainly an inexpensive
method of advertising for the ghoul.
« * *
Unlicensed distillers in and around Sydney
often get no more than 2s. or 2s. Gd. per gallon
for their whisky ; and the charges in the way of
bribing watchers, and taking bogus trips here and
there to throw excise men off the set nt, and
carrying on at a loss some bogus industry as a
blind, are so heavy that the principals, after all,
sometimes make less than a pound a week. The
publican who can absorb the stuff is the money-
maker, as he escapes the 14s. duty, and it is on
him the excise eye should be specially focussed.

A b.-each-of-promise case, Love v. Kiss, was lately tried
at Perth (W.A.)—
Many things may happen when gentlemen
leave the grocery-trade to enter upon newspaper-
management and preside over the revision of
“ society ” articles. Thus does the charming
lady-writer of Sydney Sunday Times expatiate
upon “Beds”: —
Bpdtnaking is an art which concerns everyone, and
widoli I am convinced very few women have ever taken
the trouble to acquire. Men have told me that they
would rather sit up all night than enter the beds at s ane
hotels and hoarding-houses, for apart from what Mrs.
Carlyle’s famous servant used to call “ bogues,” the
alleged places of rest are as uncomfortable as the back of
a dromedary. Many women just leave their beds as a
hare does its form, without ever turning back the bed-
clothes, or allowing the close air that has arisen from the
sleeping body to escape; or it often happens that lazy,
dirty creatures turn back the bedclothes all in a mass,
unseparated, and then, to save themselves trouble, draw
them back over the bed in the same condition. What
is the result? The bed retains an unclean and horribly
unwholesome smell, and the room is as musty as a
morgue. It will be still worse, if you persist, as I know
many of you do, in wearing in bed the woollen under-
clothes that you have had on all day, and winch
may be reeking with the effluvia of perspira-
tion. Horrible ! No, you should separate every-
thing, shake the blankets and the sheets, and
hang them in the open air, or on chairs in a good
draught, for at least half-an-hour. That precaution,
which will give a healthy tone to your room, is surely
simple. See that there are no hillocks on the bed, and do
your best with the materials at hand to make the ensemble
artistic and attractive.
The public is now looking for articles upon
“Feet ” and “ Washing your Neck,” by the same
fragrant creature.

The new tenants were accomplished artists,
and took the trouble to decorate their hired resi-
dence with yellow-astered dadoes and pretty wall-
pictures. They also “whacked in” some stun-
ning panels on the doors, and, proud of their
handicraft, they showed the landlord all the
beautifications. His full approval of the gratuitous
decoration of his premises was expressed by—
clapping on five shillings to the weekly rent. _ It
was those artists’ very first convincing political
lesson.

ciated with the “forward ” movement, says that,

On 3rd Dec., 1854, took place the storm-
ing of Eureka Stockade. No notice was taken of
the anniversary last month. Peter Lalor s grave
was undisturbe I by the dropping thereon of even
a bud. Perhaps the miners of Australasia will
now note this and act differently next 3rd Dec.

The Argus, describing recent troubles at
Greta, N.S. W., pictures the free laborers as being
compelled to display firearms to protect their lives
and limbs and avert a serious riot. The Age
describes the free laborers as brandishing revolvers
vaingloriously and nearly provoking a serious riot.
It depends upon your point of view.
# # #
A Melbourne clemi-mondaine recently
found a new way of liquidating a large and hope-
less-looking hotel bill. She beguiled a fatuous
old gentleman of good repute, and he took her to
her own hotel, where he registered as “ Mr. and
Mrs. Jones.” Next day he asked for his bill,
and found that it was .£24. “ Wha-a-at’s this?”
he asked, feebly. “We only stayed here since
yesterday.” “Yes, sir,’’ said the landlord, fixing
him with his glassy eye ; “but your wife has been
here two months. She said you would pay up
when you came.” And he paid up. It was much
cheaper than explaining.
An Auckland (M.L.) fish-dealer who lately
got into financial difficulties, assigned, as the
cause, “falling off in business since the Wairarapa
disaster.” Fish have been fat and plentiful
round the Barrier for the last few weeks, but
their fatness is gruesomely suggestive of drowned
passenger, and people look askance at them and
decline to buy. Every fish looks like a missing
friend just now,
« # #
The spring poet is everywhere. At the
very first meeting of the Wairarapa relief-fund
committee a wild-eyed person with long, spiky
hair uprose on the platform, and insisted on read-
ing “ a little thing of his own ” about the wreck.
Hesuggested that his poem should be published,
and the proceeds arising from the sale be given to
the fund, and it was necessary to drive him out
before business could be proceeded with. And yet
the short-haired Madden, 0. J., complains that we
are not a poetic people,
* * «
Dr. Hermann Kurtz, whoever he may be,
declares Albany, W.A., to be the true site of the
Garden of Eden. From which we conclude that
neither Adam nor Eve regretted eating that apple.
It was probably their only chance of getting
away.
Kiss courted Love in the moonlight pale,
And under the golden sun :
The man told ever his old, old tale
Till the fluttering heart was won.
On ripe, sweet lips he feasted high ;
Then, sated with easy bliss,
He turned away with a weary sigh
From the Love that craved a Kiss..
Love courted Kiss with a suit for “breach,”
In the humorous Hinglish style,
And the learned Judge he gazed at each
With a sorrowful, learned smile,
Whilst an angel moaned, on the roof above—
“’Twere a wonderful jury, this,
Could it reckon the damages due to Love
From that fickle deceiver, Kiss.”
But a day shall come—not yet, not yet—
In a time which counts no years,
When the Kiss of passion must pay its debt
To the Love that it left in tears.
True Love will plead for the rascal base,
And the debtor may win release
By offering then to her smiling face
The Kiss of eternal peace.
Melb.
EDMUND FISHER.

That though he no fixed income had
He fell in love —and married !
An infant, ere twelve months had sped,
Was born to destitution;
He patted baby on the head
And called him — Eetribution.

In Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, the other
evening, two men assaulted and robbed a harm-
loss passer-by, and then, with great presence of
mind, they called a policeman and gave him in
custody for _ being drunk and incapable. The
ordinary policeman generally sets down every-
body who is excited, or angry, or apoplectic, or
who has been stunned with a club, as drunk, so-
he seized the victim and cheerfully hauled him
along, despite his frantic explanations. It was
onlywhen they had gone about half-a-mile, and
the defendant had told his story for the tenth
time, that the stolid “ cop ” began to awake to a
dim perception of things and went back to look
vainly for the two strangers. They were gone,
however, and haven’t been seen since.

English Christmas literature lays such
stress on kissing under the mistletoe, that one
might be justified in imagining the mistletoe a
necessary adjunct to the osculatory rite. Yet we
in this country who have no mistletoe to kiss
under know that on© (two, for choice) can kiss
quite comfortably under a Moreton Bay fig, or
an iron bark, or a tram-shed. Yes, as long as we
have a tram-shed, blow your mistletoe!

The New Woman is troubling the Yic.
Chief-Inspector of Electoral Rolls. It was feared
that she had disguised herself in pants and in-
cipient whiskers, and taken out electoral rights
under the impression that a pair of moleskins and
three hairs on the chin were sufficient to justify
her in going to the polls. Whenever the Inspector
struck a suspicious case, he sent instructions to the
local officers to “ inquire into” the alleged elector.
One district official reported : “ L G is a
man as far as I knovy. He looks like a man and
works on tie railway.” It is manifestly easier
to give the Sex a vote than to meet these diffi-
culties.

week. All men are born equal, but inequalities
develop with startling quickness.

• • •
As in other N.S VV. State departments,
promotion in the public schools service is almost
wholly governed by seniority. Merit is, however,
recognised in the form of passing pupils through
University examinations; a success which may be
obtained in a few of the very largest schools by
assiduous coaching during routine hours. But the
great majority of teachers must gain distinction
by working the hours of thesweater. One Sydney
teacher spends two or three hours nightly all the
year round, at his private residence, in cramming
his older pupils for coveted University passes.

• # #
Quite a storm has been caused in Adelaide
by the appointment of the sister of Chief Secre-
tary Gordon to be chief of the night nurses at the
local hospital. She has only hud three years’
service, whereas several of the other nurses who
applied for the position had 14 years’ seniority.
Premier Kingston expostulated with the Board
on the subject, but they severely snubbed him;
and now he, acting on behalf of the Chief Secre-
tary, who judiciously stands aside, declines to
ratify the nomination.
« « •
“ Men who strive for money, as a means
to an end, never attain it.”
The most violent and aggressive claimants
on the Wairarapa Relief Fund, so far, have not
been poor steerage passengers and sailors, who
lost their little all and came ashore practically
naked to start the world afresh, but the large, fat,
well-to-do saloon passenger, who demands pay-
ment out of the public subscriptions for his lost
dress-suit and his bell topper. Some individuals
of this description have made the life of the relief-
committee a burden, and even threaten legal pro-
ceedings if the dress-suit and the bell-topper
aren’t replaced. The saloon passengers got all the
advt. there was in the wreck; they button-holed
all the reporters and told fairy-tales of their own
valour ; and if they could get all the relief-fund
as well they would probably be satisfied.

Sydney Worker publishes extracts from a
letter “ written by an Adelaide lady who is now' a
member of the New Australia Settlement’’—the
letter is said to be dated “Las Ovejas, Sept. 2 ”
speaking cheerily, cracking ironical jokes at the
alleged “starving” condition of the children, and
pitying by contrast the condition of those left be-
hind in Australia. It really seems unnecessary
secrecy to keep dark that writer’s name. To
whose interest among those having special favors
of audience with the Sydney Worker is it that
roseate views of New Australia should be dis-
seminated abroad ?

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was threat ning to rain as the red sun sank

And the races were over in Bidgeebel town :

Then he suddenly “dropped;” so came back—
and got tight,

“ If in town they get tight

THE BREAKER.
An Australian Scientist.
lo many an old miner “The Etheridge ” will
recall tramps under a North Queensland sun,
watches kept against wild myalls, and fossicking
over the quartz reefs; but few know that the gold-
field was named after a Sydney resident, tint
gentleman who was 1 ist week gazetted as Curator
ot the Australian Museum. Robert Etheridge
jnr was born in the purple of science, hii
father being a well - known savant attached
to the British Museum and Geological Survey,
xoung Etheridge was one of the brilliant group
who officered the first geological survey of Vic-
toria and who were widely scattered on its break-
up. Selwyn, its head, removed to Canada, where
he gained added fame as director of the Canadian
Geological Survey. Wilkinson returned toN.S.W.
and rose to be Government Geologist. Dain-
tree migrated to Queensland, where as author,
artist, and explorer he surveyed and des-
cribed the mineral wealth of the Ear North.
Before he left Australia to represent Queens-
land as Agent - General, he named a new
goldfield, “The Etheridge,” after his old Vic-
torian colleague. On losing his appointment,
Mr. Etheridge returned to Europe and obtained
employment on the geological survey of Scot-
land. Across the seas, Australia still fas-
cinated him, and his studies were frequently
directed to the solution of Australian geological
problems. In 1878 he produced “ A Catalogue of
Australian Fossils,” which still, though out of
print, is the standard work on the subject. Wider
opportunities for research were gained by his
removal to London, consequent on receiving an
appointment in the British Museum. His interest,
in Australia was kept alive by the collections:
transmitted and discoveries reported by Mr. R. L..
Jack, his Edinburgh friend and fellow-worker,,
who, by this time, had commenced his Queens-
land career. In 1887 Mr. Etheridge returned
to Australia, undertaking the joint duties of
palaeontologist to the Australian Museum and
to the geological survey of New South Wales-
which he held till the end of last year,,
when he succeeded Dr. Ramsay as curator of the
Australian Museum. At their next meeting the
Royal Society of N.S.W. will present the Clarke
medal to Messrs. Etheridge and Jack for their
investigations into Australian geology, to our
knowledge of which none have made larger con-
tributions. Besides half a dozen bulky volumes
Mr, Etheridge has published a hundred and
fifty papers on scientific topics. Most of these
deal with fossils of which few men, certainly none
south of the equator, possess so profound a know-
ledge. The ethnology of the Australian blacks is
another subject on which Mr. Etheridge ranks
as a high authority. In his new office, his
science will have full scope.
It is related that a certain Customs officer,
whilst delivering a sugar ship, looked at the sun
so often that he only chalked his weights on the
vessel’s side, and did not enter them in his book. In
the morning be discovered this, and went down tn
rectify it, but found the vessel had gone miles:
away. He followed her and found his marks in-
tact! He was previously bald, but got such a.
shock that his hair sprouted.
The fulsome eulogy of John Walter, in
the Times, is a challenge. The greatest Walter
was the second, the next the first, while the third!
was “ not in it ” with them. In fact, his bungles
reduced the status of the paper, so that it has to
share the lead of the world with half-a-dozen-
even if it be now in the fint flight. Walter did
not understand that the conduct of a great paper
requires a strong man’s whole energies. He was
a fourth-class M.P.— in itself an injury to the
limes. ihe affair of the city editor, Sampson,
was a fearful blow. Nor was the next city editor
a success. Then came the Parnell facer. Delane
was badly treated. Chenery was a thorough fish
out of water. Buckle has been ill.

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Her Majesty’s “The House that Jack
Built.”
Lyceum “Cinderella.”
Theatre Royal “ Span of Life.”
Criterion “ Hans the Boatman."
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess's Theatre “Beauty and the Beast.”
Theatre Royal “Aladdin.”
Bijou “The Judge."
At time of going to press, the Sydney
shows remained the same as last week. The four
big theatres are all open, and as the natural
result of so much theatre the cold blast of adver-
sity is beginning to blow with noiseless feet upon
one or two establishments and wither up the
takings at the front door. G. Rignold’s panto-
mime at Her Majesty’s, “The House that Jack
Built,” has caught on, however, in a fashion which
almost recalls the palmy days of old, when a soul-
less minion of the law had to be beseeched not
to fine the management for over-crowding. The
fire - act, where the ghouls and witches
dance amid a huge bonfire and come out young
again, is partly responsible for this popu-
larity ; and it is partly owing to the pretty
scene where a hundred or two of baby builders
rush up the house which Jack didn’t build after
all, and leave it completed at sunrise. There is a
ballet also which establishes a record for frank and
candid undress, and a gallery of politicians, where-
in G. Republican Dibbs appears as a thing which
is partly Hebrew old-clo’ man and partly Old
Father Time with his scalp-lock badly disarranged.
If G. Republican Dibbs wants to fully realise the
vastness of his fall he need only go and consider
this horrid presentment of himself, and realise
what the public thinks he looks like since he got
his title.
** * *
“Cinderella,” at Sydney Lyceum, con-
tinues to look a good deal brighter than it did on
the opening night, and somebody’s friend Macin-
tyre keeps breaking out in new and unexpected
spots. The two men in women’s clothes, and the
lamented death of Cock Robin are still to the front,
and Miss Ray Jones has got over her preliminary
nervousness—or, at least, over a good deal of it —
and tackles her part in a more effective fashion
than she did at first. And everyone you meet in
the street is humming the tuneful tenor’s “Sweet
Marie.”
** * *
“Hans the Boatman,” with the old
favorite, Charles Arnold, as the poetic owner of
the big dog, will be staged at Sydney Criterion on
Saturday. “Hans ”is for six nights only, and, so
far, there is no definite announcement as to further
arrangements.
** * *
Bland Holt is doing well with the “ Span
of Life” at Sydney Royal, and a change of pro-
gramme is still afar off. The camels, the realistic
steamboat which takes a bee-line for destruction
on a dark night, the lighthouse, the Arabs, and
the other glories of the show are all in good health
and doing well. The “Span of Life ” lays over
all the other melodramas which The Bulletin
can remember just now in the matter of realism,
and next time Bland Holt is faced with the eternal
necessity for going one better he will have a par-
ticularly hard row to hoe,
** * *
R. S. Smythe writes that our correspon-
dent “G.,” in last issue, is right in stating that
Mrs. Annie Besant gave her share of the profits of
her Australian tour to the Theosophical Institute
in London, but in putting her share of the Sydney
lectures at £79, “ G.” is very unjust to the people
of Sydney, who patronised Mrs. Besant’s 10 lec-
tures so liberally that her share averaged .£3O per
night. “ G.’s” £79 odd was what she got for her
four extra lectures, when the gilt was off the
gingerbread. So long as the Theosophical Insti-
tute has a member who can earn money for them
at this rate, it would surely be madness for W,
Q. Judge, or any other correspondent of the
Mahatmas, to think of expelling her.
** * *
Bulletin readers, and the theatrical
public generally, will be shocked to hear that
Miss Myra Kemble’s illness appears to be much
more serious than at first anticipated. She is
pronounced to be suffering from internal cancer,
and her relatives have no hope of an ultimate
recovery. The loss of this bright and clever
actress to the Australian stage would be greatly
felt, and The Bulletin sincerely hopes that the
worst fears are unfounded.
** * *
Since the Princess’ (Melb.) panto, was
shortened it seems to consist more than ever of an
aggressive humorist named Daddy, played by
Alfred Saker. Other items were cut down, but
no reduction in Daddy has been accomplished.
Daddy’s popularity is really based on baggy
trousers and bladders concealed about his person,
although his bony legs also find great favor when
he appears in undress, with a rug strapped
round his middle. He changes his clothes and toils
unceasingly. In the opinion of all who can laugh
at this industrious comedian, and have not heard
his songs, or other versions of them, sung by Will
Whitburn in days of yore, “Beauty and the
Beast ” is a successful pantomime. Even those
who don’t find Saker a great source of merriment
may at least feel grateful for the timely suppres-
sion of two other imported funny men. The couple
in question maddened their first night audience
by talking, for ten solid minutes, about a family
that died in bed, and a mother-in-law who suffered
from large feet, after which they defied the masses
with a melancholy duet. They are now reduced
to the ranks, so to speak, and the fact that they
came from a distant country to do something is
quite lost sight of. Six characters in this enter-
tainment follow Daddy around in single file, like
the tail of a comet. Miss Polly Emery, sad to
say, is a portion of the tail aforesaid. The clever
little woman is naturally funnier than ten panto-
mimes put together, yet she is doomed to hide her
true character in the background.
* • *
Miss Marie Luella’s legs are a pair of
heavenly twins, also her voice is mellow, and all
that she does is done handsomely, but one must
admit that the dear “ boy ” might do a little more
without wearing herself out. Miss Alice Saker,
as Beauty, is a fairly bright music-hall inrjinue,
who expresses artless simplicity with a ta-ra-ra
smile; whilst the dancing of her young friends,
the village children, is the prettiest incident in
the show. The Ballet of Nations goes very well,
and the living pictures, which at first were
“skied ” in the transformation-scene, so that only
their calves caught the eye, have been re-arranged
by artist Gordon and the hanging-committee.
** * *
“ Aladdin,” at the Royal, has three strong
points in its favor It is the best of all pantomime
subjects for incidental display; the deep stage
gives the spectacles plenty of elbow-room, and the
audiences are immense. A crowded house is a
stimulant to a show of the purely Xmas kind.
The company throw off their feeblest topical allu-
sions with easy confidence, and a roar of applause
encourages them to deeds which they wouldn’t
dare to commit in cold blood. Consequently,
“ Aladdin ” becomes more lively every night. It
is the right thing in the right place, with the right
Maggie Moore at the head of affairs, ready to
meet any emergency. There can be no stagna-
tion where Maggie Moore is concerned, and the
value of her festive personality on occasions like
this is just as great as ever it was.
** * *
George Darrell’s season at the Opera House
didn’t stretch further than Saturday night. “ The
Nightmare,” which probably came from America,
is a wildly elaborated nigger farce, with the burnt
cork rubbed off its surface. Throughout three
short acts the members of George’s company were
either chasing him in the hope of getting married
(he being a matrimonial agent), or lodging com-
plaints against a gouty person, who exploded an
infernal machine in their midst. George himself
had a romantic side to his character, as shown in
his habit of writing verses on sheeps’“ jimmies.”
Another tender soul was the bald-headed waiter,
who wanted no dowry with his bride, but ex-
pected her to come provided with a change of
“unders.” Had “The Nightmare” been a
reasonable comedy, it was quite naughty enough
to run for three months.
** * *
“ The Judge,” at Melb. Bijou, holds out
much better than The Bulletin expected, and
the wail of the wax baby in the next room is heard
every evening until further notice. Situated be-
tween the two pantomimes “The Judge”seems to
fill a want. Moreover, the Bijou prices are
“ popular,” and the aroma of past successes clings
to William Elton’s name. For that reason,
perhaps, Elton is gradually breaking down
the barrier between one of .Her Majesty’s
Judges, and Lurcher, the Dorothian bailiff. Any-
way, Sir John Pye, flattering a policeman’s
mother in “ The Judge,” might as well be Lurcher
paying his addresses to Mrs. Privett. The
policeman’s ma happens to be Mrs. Bryer, and
that lady assists in bringing “Dorothy ” back to
one’s mind. Miss Clara Thompson, who invades
the Judge’s lodgings after midnight and compels
him to nurse her infant, errs a good deal
on the side of extravagance, but even she is
a mild offender compared with Elton. For
the rest, Wilfrid Shine, as the baby’s male parent,
is not very happily placed, but the Judge’s
daughters—Miss Harrie Ireland and Miss
Katherine Hardy—are deliciously sweet and
natural. Also, their innocence is such that when
their supposed step-mother produces the baby as
evidence that she married their papa a fortnight
ago, the artless young creatures merely clap their
hands and cry “ How nice!”
At Poverty Point.
The question with the lady patrons of “ The Judge,”
Melb. Bijou, is whether Elton wears his own legs.
Geo. Coppin, jun., acts in “ Under the Clock,” Melb.
Oxford. Can’t keep ’em off.
That excellent actor, Baker, has so long languished in
the slough of the villains, like Macready, Irving, Willard,
and Sass, that it is a treat to see how well he can do .the
lead.
Bouoicault, sen., prided himself on his likeness to
Shakespeare, but did not attain such a resemblance as
Pitt, the Melb. scenic artist and boniface, whose son
designed the Princess’.
Dear Bulletin,— No wonder “ The Firm” occasionally
imports some mediocre foreign talent, for the locally-
grown article has a rough road to travel. For instance—
I wrote a song, which was sung for two weeks with great
success at a Sydney house. Everyone who has heard it
has a good word for it. Despite this, the manager would
not print the name of the composer in the programme,
and not a paper—weekly or daily—gave even the name of
the song. The second week found it going so well that
it was kept on, but only one weekly paper gave the title
of the local song, while all the British productions were
named in full. If my song had been a failure 1 should
have been pleased with this conspiracy of silence, but as
it is successful beyond the average “colonial” production
I think they might have printed its name, especially as I
sent copies for review. lam not trying for a cheap ad.
from you, but would be pleased if you called attention to
the fact. —P.A. Melb., Dec. 28, ’94.
Maggie Moore will play a burlesque and dramatic season
in Sydney before departing for the States.
“ Popular ” prices at Coolgardie—6s., 55., 3s.
One of the most popular of Australian theatrical
agents discarded his family name (Bastard) because
it didn’t sound nice. Nevertheless, it is the name of one
of the “ best ” county families in Devonshire, Eng.
“ Bob ” Brough generously gave £5O to the Wairarapa
relief-fund, stipulating that the fact should not be
announced until after his Maoriland season was over.
And now—for thus is virtue ever rewarded—dear old
Robert gets two advts. —one for his good-nature and the
other for his modesty.
The Dacres are bringing at least five plays with them
“A Bunch of Violets,” “Man and Woman,” “A Nine
Days’ Wonder,” “The Double Marriage,” and “Esther
Sandraz.” In the last named, which contains a specially
emotional part for Mrs. Amy Roselle-Dacre, they will
probably open their Australian campaign. By the way,
the Dacres are that rarest of professional combinations,
“a devoted couple.” Both husband and wife have refused
tempting offers to join companies that could only accom-
modate one, without the other.
A movement is afoot in Melbourne to send clever little
Percy Grainger to Europe for his musical education. The
idea is to get a number of people to contribute ss. a year
each for four years. The sum wanted is from £lOO to
£l5O a year ; which guaranteed, the boy and his mother
will set off for Germany.

The cricketers who answered Geo. Darrell’s cordial in-
vitation to appear in the race-scene in “ The Double
Event” disguised themselves in various ways with burnt
cork, colonial beer, &o. The public were much dis-
appointed, as nothing like a cricketer was discernible
from the front.
Saker, the new Princess’s pantomimist, is, to a con-
siderable extent, a tailor-made comedian. It probably
takes nine tailors to make this humorist. He wears a
fresh suit every time he comes on, and each suit is more
preposterous than the other, one of his •• finest effects ”
being produced by wearing blown bladders in the bosom
of his pants. Saker must have a very modest dresser, or
the latter would insist upon stepping out and acknow-
ledging some of the applause.
A certain Melb. veteran simply puts two touches with a
burnt cork under his nose and is “made up” for any part
for which he is cast.
The almost defunct Melb. Lyric Club, which some weeks
ago arranged a 50 per cent, compromise with its creditors,
is now giving its last kicks on the downward grade, by
paying 6s. Bd. in the £ on its remaining liabilities.
Some talk of Geo. Rignold taking a dramatic co. to
Maoriland shortly.
Surgeon O’Hara, bruiser and vocalist, seems to have
taken up the London concert-hall plan of having a round
of enjoyments the same day. On Xmas Day he sang
before Mass at a Melb, Cathedral, and then started off for
Carlton church, where he was in time to give a solo at
the collection.
A very up-to-date Yankee manager is, for a lOOOdol.
wager, going to tour his co. from New York to ’Frisco on
bicycles, ladies and all. The “ heavy lead ”on a small
safety would be delicious. Whether or not the co., who
will have to work all day as well as at night, are to parti-
cipate in the profits, isn’t known.
Those “negro minstrel” shows in England and America
which have adhered to the original style of male per-
formers only, still do big business. The decay of this
class of entertainment seems to have set in with the intro-
duction of female artistes. It is like introducing waitresses
to a male-waiter restaurant.
The lady star of the Paris music-halls now shows the
art of gracefully going to bed, before the audience. It
was bound to come.

London County Council. Barrister Gill elicited, in cross-
exam., that Mrs. H. was separated from her husband.
George Edwardes, manager of London Gaiety and
Empire theatres, strongly objects to the representation
of nude figures in Living Pictures. He thinks that their
only attraction for “ those who care to see them ” depends
upon the idea of indecency. Moral George Edwardes !
The most important branch of the business of his theatri-
cal life has been the procuring of shapely chorus-girls
how many of them kept “ straight ” ?—for the purpose of
displaying them in burlesque costumes that suggest
nudity to anyone who seeks suggestiveness. A motion-
less figure of a girl posed to represent an artistic study
from the nude is quite as wholesome a spectacle as a
young lady dancing and glancing in a costume which
barely fills a glove-box.

An erstwhile well-known “ romantic ” actress, lately
entered a Case lor legal separation against her husband,
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent, but the
case has been withdrawn. The hubby is a rich Man-
chester man, who, to afford his wife an opportunity of
distinguishing herself, built a theatre in London, and
presented it to her, but she could not make a hit. She
produced a ghastly bad play, which was hissed off
the stage. On that occasion she came forward, and with
tears in her eyes, and in her best tragedy manner, asked
the audience if they wished her to continue or not. The
reply was emphatic, though no bricks were thrown.

about a rainy three years? ”

The new Lady Godiva will ride through
Coventry on a bicycle. An idea for the English
