London Times fatuously suggests that
Premier Turner should go slow with the land-tax,
as the producing interest is already taxed to
breaking-point. The impudence of the suggestion
that the Vic. land-monopolist is a producer of any-
thing but personal Eat is duly appreciated by
everybody but the asinine cable-man.
The worst point about that astounding
return of deadheads on the N.S.W. railways is
the unabashed manner in which alleged democrats
have taken advantage of their Parliamentary
position to bleed the taxpayer on behalf of their
female relations. From the Barbours and Melvilles
and Willises one expects nothing better; but the
Cottons and Raes and Traills, without mentioning
the Blacks, Cooks and Bavisters, or the Scheys,
Eddens and Fergusons, should be the first to dis-
courage a system which has no shadow of justifica-
tion, and lends itself so easily to flagrant abuse.
For how can aM.P., whose wife or daughter travels
free over the province’s railways, protest with
force against the same privilege being extended to
unspecified visitors who can work backstairs
influence to save their pockets? What business
has Mrs. Hoyle to travel free any more than
“ Mrs. Wood, daughter of late C. Cowper ?”
Have Miss Danahey’s unpaid journeys any better
justification than those of “Mrs. Wollaston,”
“Miss Sanderson,” and “Miss M. Leakie,” who
omitted to pay their fares like ordinary people
because they were “visitors” who naturally
preferred to save their money when they
could find a State railway department
ass enough to carry them about for the
honor of the thing ? Apparently, about 600
various females, more or less related to Ms.P. of
all the provinces of Australasia, or to English
officials, or to the English “aristocracy,” have
been using the N.S.W. railways as they felt
inclined, without paying a cent, while the common
variety of taxpayer and his wife and the
hard-up seamstress or governess, dump their
fares down regularly to keep the trains running,
and are prosecuted with great vigor if they ride
without a ticket. The poor public 1
SPOUTING NOTIONS.
An impartial outsider wou'd say that the result
of the two test matches goes to show the Australians have
not got the Englishmen’s stamina. They are not so
reliable, so consistent, as their cold-climate cousins.
More brilliant in patches, their brilli nicy can never lie
depended on at a crisis ; their “form ” is,here to-day and
gone to-morrow, while the Englishmen rarely fail to piny
up to concert Ditch. “It’s dogged as does it.’’ Then
some of the Australians play to the pavilion, the bar-
raokers, and the reporters ; the Englishmen invariably
play to win, and sink individual success in the success of
the team. And they are not handicapped by having three
bosses in three provinces, with three sets of prejudices
and preferences. This by way of explanation, not of
excuse.
• • • »
The fiction that English “test” teams would
always prefer to meet the Australians on a sodden
wicket was invented by a patriotic press to suit the open-
ing of the Melbourne match. It is an undeniable fact
that the British “cracks,” from Grace downwards, are
fair-weather batsmen, and that the Australians, com-
pelled to learn, on hard ground, to howl with their heads,
are superior to the more mechanical pros, who are in-
dulged with so many bowlers’ wickets in their own damp
country. The Australians in England have never abso-
lutely won a test-match on a batsmen’s wicket, but they
have twice triumphed in the mud.
• * * »
Another Byron Moore is wanted to take the
Melh. Cricket Club ground in hand and make a pretty
show-place of it. The shilling part is a badly-asphalted
slope, furnished with an absurdly small amount of dirty
seating accommodation, and on the other side of this
semi-circle hill there Is nothing but squalid chaos. The
scoring-board and box, and adjoining circumstances, are
not a hit like what they might he if the M.O.C. “author-
ities" had less gas and more go.
• * • *
The failure of a S.A. batsman against the
Englishmen was prophesied by intimates who heard him
talk. In his last innings against Stoddart’s men he
got double figures, and said, “I’m in good form;”
whereas, in one innings against Grace and Co., he hit up
149, and afterwards exclaimed, “Some day I’ll, make
1000; I'm as full of runs as a dog is of fleas.” With the
loss of the art of run-making, he has also lost the gift of
eloquence. He should take a spell.
* * * •
S.A. Cricketing Association was asked by
Melh. Cricket Club and Sydney Ground Trustees to join
in the risk accompanying Stoddart’s team, but the
S.A.C.A. d dined. The receipts up to and including the
Melh. test match have, however, paid all expenses of the
tour, and the two bodies expect to divide over £7OOO
profits next April. This news will be very pleasing to
the Adelaide body.
» » * *
Let us look facts calmly in, the face. The
Englishmen have won the first two test matches. In the
first match the Australians had the rosiest of rosy
chances —and did not win ; the English had the barest of
bare chances and did win—although their howlers, it is
true, were given their opportunity on a sticky wicket.
In the second test-match the Australians were set 428 to
win in their second innings ; the English had scored 475
in their second. The Australians, on a run-getting wicket,
failed ; with 3 for 206, a rot set in, relieved only by the
game batting of Iredale, who scored 68, and when Iredale
was at length bowled by Peel the innings closed 94 behind
the English score. Giffen admitted that the better team
had won ; it would indeed he hard for him to admit any-
thing else with the grandly consistent hatting of the
English staring him in the face ; for Richardson, who
made 11, was their lowest scorer. If the English retain
their present form, it seems any odds that they will come
out of the test matches with the supremacy. .Meanwhile,
the Sydney, Melbourne, and Adelaide cricket associations
had better fly an advt. from the flag-staffs of their respec-
tive pavilions: “Wanted, a phenomenal. Australian
bowler.”
• • * *
Lockwood was brought to book by the crowd
during the Australians’ Melh. second innings. After
delivering the ball he would, apparently without neces-
sity, run up the pitch, which, already worn after four
days’ play, was not improved by this conduct. That this
was a fact was proved by nine of the Australians being
dismissed from that end.
♦ ♦ ♦
On the penultimate day of the second test-
match the climax of woe tor Australia was reached
when Trumble got run out. Trumble had played a palter-
ing game all along, hut at the ignoini nous end, to an un-
pleasant innings the hearts of the hqrrackers stopped
with a sickening thud. Wicket after Wicket had fallen,
but not till this awful moment did despair take full
possession. A sporting Englishman offered 10 to Ito a
jockey standing near him. “ Bah !’’ said the jockey,
“it’s hell to 1.” . . E’en the ranks of Tuscany fore-
bore to cheer—the Britishers, present in sufficient num-
bers, looked on joyfully, hut their joy was chastened with
pity. Australia seemed to have defeat and disgrace
assured—and then Iredale and Turner rose with healing
on their wings and staved off the disgrace.
* * » •
It has been reserved for the M.C.C. to put up a
record for cricket trickery. The M.C.C. is an alleged
gentlemen’s club—purse-proud and arrogant. In its
match against the East Melh. C.C. it was afraid to
play the game on its merits —it preferred tactics. Fri-
day’s heavy rains made the wickets sticky and almost un-
playable—M.C.C. won the toss and sent in E.M.C.C. to but.
So far, so good. E.M.C.C. were put out for 91—and
M.C.C. went in to bat. When 5 wickets were down for
61, and the wicket terrible, M.C.C. closed its innings and
thus forced E.M.C.C. in again to play in a bad light on a
wicket the M.C.C. shirked. The object all sublime of
M.C.C. was, if possible, to force its opponents to play two
innings on a bowler’s wicket, its own crowd being con-
tent with half an innings on a bad wicket and the big
chance of a good wicket on the next Saturday. By a
wrench of rules M.C.C. thus obtained for itself a second
option after winning the toss. In the second innings 5
of the best E.M.C.C. batsmen have been got out by the
ruse, but it is to be seriously hoped that the remainder
will score heavily enough to defeat the most miserable
and unmanly cricket subterfuge ever attempted in Aus-
tralia.
• * * *
Nobody begrudged Brockwell a bowling success
in the Melbourne match. His comparative failure as a
batsman on Australian wickets had begun to dispirit the
Surrey crack, and the pleasure of finding himself a heavy
scorer with the ball was extra sweet. In his little
luncheon speech, Brockwell modestly referred to himself
as an emergency bowler, but the last Australian Eleven
in England saw cause to think highly of his attack.
* * ♦ »
What are the odds about all the Englishmen
getting double-figures in their next innings against Aus-
tralia? Pretty considerable. Nevertheless, they are
clearly a pluckier team when put to it than the overrated
Sheffield Eleven of three years ago, and Sloddart is a
cleverer tactician, on and off the field, than W. G. Grace
proved himself. '

Corbett says he is satisfied Steve O’Donnell

* * * *
The old yacht Australian, better known to old-
time Sydney boating-men as the “Beaky," has been
broken up. The Australian was built upwards of 40 years
ago by Dan Shea, and when Walter Reeks, the English
naval architect, first came to Sidney and saw her model,
he said that the Austialian was the boat the Britishers
had been aiming at for a lifetime. The old Beaky was a
freak, and to the day of her smashing up nothing local or
imported, appr Mching her tonnage, had a show with
her.
* ♦ * *
Old Boccaccio, the imported trotter owned by
the popular Mr. “'Ted ” Underwood (killed by a fall from
his horse, on Christmas morning, at his Quirindi station),
would not now be recognised as the gay stallion who
used to delight Sydney horsemen by his pace and action.
Mr. Underwood had of late years been driving the old
fellow in his buggy, and on appearance he would not
bring a fiver in a saleyard.

Mr. Humphrey Oxenham, one of the largest,
best-known and most strictly reliable bookmakers in or
out of Australasia, has gone into the consultation busi-
ness at Tattersall’s Club, Brisbane, and has sweeps
op mon all current horsey events of importance. Also,
he is prepared to lay singles or doubles or trebles, and to
do all other business in his line. The Brisbane address is
a matter of necessity, the Cold Tea fanatics who boss
Sydney Post Office having ukased that all letters directed
to sweep-promoters or other wicked gamblers in N.S.W.
shall be detained and returned to the senders. This pro-
ceeding hasn’t stopped or even seriously reduced gamb-
ling; it only compels straight sportsmen like Oxenham
to shift into the adjacent province, and makes a woeful
gap in the N.S. VV. postal revenue.

Dear Bulletin, —In a recent big consultation a
man and his two mates drew a horse owned by a Central
Q. owner, to whom they wired, offering him “ £lOOO to
nothing first.” Owner wired back in reply: “Am not
satisfied—can do better here. Want £l5OO first, £5OO
second, £3OO third. Reply through ” the sweep.
prom iter). They were hard-up, and so laid him what
he asked. Now, that wire of his laid before the stewards
of the club would surely have called forth the pertinent
query: “How was he going to do better?” Would it
not? Pleasant thing for the sweep-subscribers, this black-
mail by owners of horses. And isn’t it likely to harm the
sweep-promoter in the future? Plenty of people, the
writer included, would have replied to the owner: “Do
better and be !”— Subscriber.
The Bulletin understands that the owner of
one horse, which was a very hot favorite for Melb. Cup,
demanded a clear half of any first, second, or third prize-
money that might be won by the drawers of his horse in
the two biggest Tattersnll gambles. The penalty for non-
compliance with this request was, it is said, to be the
scratching of the “ moral.”
“Subscriber, ” it will be noted, does not say that
the sweep-promoter gave any information whatever as to
who held the ticket, but the incident is another clear
proof, were proof needed, that whenever drawers’ names
and addresses are disclosed to horseowners the door to
intimidation and blackmail is opened. In a word, dis-
closure reduces the value of 20s. worth of big chance in a
sweep to 10s., less 10 per cent. If the sweep-promoter
stipulated in his circular for a half-share of the place
prizes, his rapacity would seem shameful. Yet it is all the
same to the ticket-purchasers, taking them as a specula-
tive body, whether the sweep-boss or the owner levies this
tax. In a 100,000 sweep, Jones or Brown takes odds of
99,999 to 1 against winning either of the three stated
sums, with mmor contingencies in his favor, and if those
sums are to be sweated as aforesaid, the value of his
investment is reduced accordingly.
Owing to the policy of ticket-drawers in throw-
ing offers at owners’ heads by way of inducing them to
start their horses, some owners almost persuade them-
selves that they have an honest claim upon sweep moneys
—as though the lucky ticket-holders were outside backers
who had stepped in and spoilt the betting-market for
i hem. In the latter case, an owner has some sort of
e i lev ance against the man who stands to win a fortune
upon his horse—in a sweep case he has none whatsoever.
-Sweeps tend to feed themnarket instead of starving it, by
giving the lucky ones a chance to lay-off a hit” to the
books at a liberal price. A horse-owner should properly
have no more to do with a sweep-ticket-holder than
w ith the man in the moon.
In sweeps, we, the public, are gambling among
ourseWes. We agree to pay the swer p-promoter two
shillings in the & as commission, and draw lots for shares
in the remaining’ 90 per cent, of our pool. Horses are not
necessary to the gamble. We could run our spec, if we
hked on German-lottery lines, and considering that the
iuif, take it altogether, is a sinful matter, the public
ought not to let their lotteries encourage the breeding of
dead-birds. ■ A lottery which yields a subsidy to horse-
racing is twice as unwholesome as it need be.
* * • •
The “ sohlenter ” sweep-promoter in Banana-
land follows the example of the reconstructing banker.
He only promises to pay. The police see the little games
going on, but wait calmly in their offices pending “ in-
structions.”

Saturday, when the first four horses home in the Tatter-
sail s Cup were found to be the identical four that finished
first in the Summer Cup. There was only one exception
in the matter of placing, Florrie and The Trier changing

Dick Burge has been taught by bitter experi-
ence that it is not wise to give too much weight aw'ay,
especially when opposed to a man like Ted Pritchard, who
simply knocked him out in . ight minutes. Pritchard
fought savagely, audit looked as if there was some bad
blood between the men. There was a long delay wrangling
about a referee, and this did Burge no good, as he was a
bit on the light side and looked very pale. Eventually
Joe Steers, the quasi-amateur boxer, was chosen by con-
sent of both men, but all he had to do was to count
Burge out. A lame and impotent conclusion. It is
singular, remarks The Bulletin’s London correspondent,
what a bad judge of a glove-fighting man old Jem Mace
is. In fact, most good fighters are so. I would back Joe
Thompson s selection against Mace’s and give consider-
able odds. Once again Jem was on the side of the loser.

Dear Bulletin, — He Beckwith’s over-arm swim-
ming “ The mode of swimming among the Mandans, as
well as among most of the other tribes, is quite different
from that practised in those parts of the civilised woild
which I have had the pleasure yet to visit. The Indian,
instead of parting his hands simultaneously under the
chin and making the stroke outwardly in a horizontal
direction, causing thereby a serious strain upon the chest,
throws his body alternately upon the left and the right
side, raising one arm entirely above the water and reach-
ing as far forward as he can to dip it, whilst his whole
weight and force are spent upon the one that is passing
under him and like a paddle propelling him along. Whilst
this arm is making a half-circle and is being raised out of
the water behind him, the opposite arm is describing a
similar arch in the air over his head, to be dipped in the
water as far as he can reach before him, with the hand
turned under, forming a sort of bucket, to act most effec-
tively as it passes in its turn underneath him. By this
bold and powerful mode of swimming, which may want
the grace that many would wish to see, I am quite sure
from the experience I have had, that much of the fatigue
and strain upon the breast and spine are avoided, and that
a man will preserve his strength and his breath much
longer in (his alternate and rolling motion than he can in
the usual mode of swimming in the polished world.”—
(Gatlin’s Letters on the North American Indians.)—
Q. W. M.
♦ * * ♦
J. G. Kennedy, the retired champion amateur
sculler of N.S.W., is one of the few men w’ho loved train-
ing and whom training loved. Out of condition he scaled
41b. less than when in the pink of form ; with him to
train down was to train up. He could set any man at
rest who doubts the encouragf raent given to Sydney
scullers. He won the amateur championship four times
in succession, viz., from ’B7 to ’9O inclusive, and as win-
ning this event three times in succession meant securing
permanently the Sydney Mail Gup, Kennedy claimed it
from the Rowing Association. They, or “someone,” had
however, lost it; but they promised Kennedy a 25-guinea
trophy in lieu of it. They also promised him a medal for
beating six other competitors in the amateur champion-
ship sculls of ’BB. These promises have not been re-
deemed. Now that there is a (baby) Kennedy, jun. the
association might get ready in time to bequeath’ the
trophy to the retired champion’s heir-at-law.
* * * *
Gov. Gormanston (Tas.) tells this story: “I
was down in Bally (something or other), a small country
town in Ireland, to see a race-meeting, and everything
passed off very satisfactorily until, close to the finish of
the principal event, the jockey on the leading horse, see-
ing No. 2 coming up rapidly behind him, deliberately
crossed, bringing the rear man almost to a standstill and
then galloped in first. To my amazement, up his name
went as winner. I sought the judge, whom I knew very
well, for an explanation. ‘ Well,’ said he, ‘lt was a cross
ahure enough, but, ye see, it’s this way—the man who did
it comes down here every year and gives us a hundred
whoile the other feller’s niver been here before.’ ‘ And 5
added His Ex., ‘I was so damned disgusted that I’ve never
been near the blessed place since !’ ”
• * * *
Sydney Truth’s new missing-word competition
is a very safe offer. You have to guess right twice in the
same sentence, with anything up to about 5000 more or
less suitable words in each case. What odds for the
double with 10,000 starters going blind, and nothing
known of form or condition ? And even if you do <r e t one
leg in you never hear about it. The gambler who responds

* * ♦ *
The Commission de Repartition du Pari Mutuel
has just distributed amongst various charities in France
the large sum of 2,675,000 francs, being the deduction of
10 per cent, from the amount staked in the machine
during the past racing season. Thus do the practical
French combine betting with benevolence, an example
which might he followed with advantage in Australia
wherever the “ tote ”is permitted. £107,000 would keep
a good many paupers. *
* * * *
Jem Smith, the burly English boxing fraud, is
being resuscitated. A match between Smith and Frank
Slavin, at latest, was all but arranged.
» * * «
Admirable as most of the Randwick arrange-
ments are, there is one improvement sorely needed—a
large board legibly displaying the numbers of the starters
and names of their jockeys. If placed at the bottom of
the lawn adjoining the Leger Reserve it would be visible
both to the people on the stands and to the long-suffering
flattites, who now have to take the starters on trust from
the bookies. The cost of erecting such a board would be
but trifling, whilst the boon would be immensely appre
mated by race goers of all classes.

The Akqus reports that a meeting advertised to
take place at Shepparton, Vic., to condemn the Land-tax,
was only attended by 12 persons “owing to the opening
of the duck-season.” But the country is a-flame against
“the obnoxious tax.” The Argus prevaricates with much
clumsiness.
» * » *
Thousands of people will be glad to hear that
the straight and manly “Billy” Guesdon, erstwhile of
Warwick Farm Racing Club, Sydney, has lately had a
substantial piece of financial good fortune. It is related
of the gentle William that long ago he and a friend of his
were made J.’sP. and went to court to get their hands in
Judicially. Almost the first case called concerned cruelty to
a horse. William’s sporting blood was aroused at once, and
his friend was a sportsman, too. “ Look here, prisoner,”
said W. Guesdon, J.P., “you are a hopeless ruffian. If
you had half-killed a man as big as yourself in a fair row j
—well, the court might have had some sympathy, but to
thus treat a helpless horse is unpardonable. It is a case
Of ten pounds or three months. Next time it will be a
year without the option.” Then they felt better, and
proceeded to lunch. Prisoner, in default of pay-
ment, had been removed to gaol, and his disconsolate
family now came round to beg the beaks for mercy.
The end of the matter is, of course, obvious to anybody'
acquainted with W. Guesdon, J.P. He and his friend i
paid the fine out of their own pockets.
• • » *
Writes “Victor”: — Blarneystone stands out
prominently as the most successful holiday race-per-
former. This full brother to Marvel has improved latterly
out of recollection, and his wins in the two Cups place
him amongst the best handicap horses in Australia. Like
old Highborn he has blossomed late in life, and, to carry
the simile farther, he will, if not weight-crushed, emulate
that grand old black’s performances by accounting for
some of the more classic of Australian races. J. Tierney
deserves praise for the masterly way in which he rode
Blarneystone in all his races. There is no “flashness”
about his riding, and, as he uses his head well, there
should be a bright prospect before him.
Tridentate’s running in both Cups was to me in-
explicable. With that able hors°man, James Gough, in
the saddle, he never once showed prominently in Tatt.’s
Cup, though he went out a strong second favorite. How-
ex er, I don’t yet mean to absolutely sponge him out.
Donation, after running last for part of the
journey, finished up fourth in Tatt.’s Cup, and as he is
now getting into rare fettle he may shortly be returned a
winner.
Ruenalf wants a spell. ’Twas deplorable to see
this really grand horse finish absolutely' last in the two-
mile race. Owners should remember that horses are not
machines.
That grand mare, Florrie, ran very consistently
in both Cups, and no one would have begrudged Walter
Hall a win with her in either of them. At the gale of
y earlings at Wellington Park, near Auckland (M.L.), a
full brother to her—a chestnut colt by Tasman —Rubina—
was sold for 45g5.!
Pharamond is getting into form : he ran well in
both Carrington Stakes and New Year’s Gift.
Havilah White’s successes with Georgio, Ti-
woona, and Form were well received and, moreover, well
deserved.
Vanitas and Blarneystone were two nasty
knocks for the ring, but otherwise the fielders had, as per
usual, much the best of the game.
Ed. Bulletin, — A large section of the
press denounces the N.S. W. railway-free pass
system which of late has been so extensively
abused by influential ladies. But tell me—why
should newspaper-people be allowed to travel half-
fare ? A newspaper is only a private speculation.
The emissaries of the papers pay only half-fare;
the ladies pay nothing. Both systems are vicious
in principle.—Joss.
Dear Bulletin, —I unwittingly misled you
when I said that J. A. Hill was the sole survivor
of the little band of pilgrims who watched Gov.
Hind marsh proclaim S.A. a British province 58
years ago. I find that R. G. Symonds, H. T.
Morris, and half-a-dozen other ancient identities,
were “round the sacred spot” on Glenelg Beach
on the eventful 28th Dec., 1836. The survivors of
great battles and of historic ceremonies always
bob up in the most unexpected places. The fact
is, I took old Mr. Hill’s word, without testing his
accuracy. At least, he is the most picturesque of
the band of septuagenarians and worse. He
helped his namesake, the boatswain on H.M.S.
Buffalo, to hoist the flag and, being a printer’s
apprentice, he assisted in setting up the official
proclamation published on' the occasion.—Yours,

When Miss Adelaide Blank recently
married a S.A. newspaper canvasser, “I wasn
sure,” sighed the bridegroom to a congratulatory
friend, “that I had got my ad. till the contract
was signed.”
J. A. Andrews, the anarchist, charged at
Sydney with seditious libel, is in his thirtieth
year, and claims to be of English, French, Ger-
man, Scandinavian, Spanish, Arabian, African
and Hebrew extraction. All these nationalities
fused themselves into a grandfather who was a
baronet and a father who was chief clerk in an
important Australian public department.
Melb. Argus demands a further lavish
outlay on “our defences,” on the ground that, if
some foreign enemy successfully raided Victoria,
“we should lose our good name and our self-
respect. We should feel that we were not worthy
members of the valiant British race.” Apparently
the only good name is the name of someone who
has successfully mashed a foreign enemy, and the
advantage of having a good name is that it makes
us feel we are members of the British race. The
idea of having a good name and being members of
the Australian race isn’t even mentioned.
There seems at last to be a fair prospect
of the Field of Mars (Sydney) tramway scheme
being carried out. To a recent deputation Min-
ister Young made a distinct promise that, if the
work could be done for less than ,£20,000, it would
be carried out, whereat the far-sighted speculators
and syndicates who purchased the land are
jubilant. It appears that some eight years ago,
when the land was sold, the auctioneers made a
kind of promise that the Govt, would build a
tramway to connect with the metropolis, either
via the G.N, railway or the Parramatta River.
As a consequence the land was bought at a good
price, not by genuine settlers, who might use the
tram, but by speculators in unearned increment.
For some reason or other the tram was not built,
and a Field of Mars tramway deputation has
howled on the Ministerial door-mat ever since. If
the line is built it will never pay, as the district
is too far removed from the metropolis for suburban
population and is already sufficiently catered for
by the G.N. railway. This is one of the many
cases where a Betterment Act would come in
with advantage.
Justice is muzzled revenge.
A Northern Moses has got badly taken
down by-a varnish-manufacturer, who hypothe-
cated a large quantity of his output. When the
crash came, Moses found himself left with a lot
of varnish that had been specially manufactured
for hypothecation. Now, if asked to give evidence
before the Tariff Commission on the varnish
industry, his remarks become unfit for publication.
They are again clamouring for the New
Guinea variety of nigger-slave up North. The
dear old kanaka kind has taken to firing on re-
cruiting-boats, by way of showing how he loves to
come and be kicked in the neck by planters’ over-
seers ; and there has arisen a warm desire to
clothe, and Christianise, and civilise the savage
New Guinea brother, and teach him “how to
cultivate the cane which will some day be the
staple industry of his island.” But Administrator
Macgregor rather sniffs at the charity which
begins so far away, from home, and the scheme
languishes.

Sydney Wool Sales.
M'Burmsv, Wallis & Co. report.—On Thursday, 3rd
i'isr.., we oil red a catalogue of small lots only, at.d found
competition very brisk, with prices equal to rates lately
ruling. The advantage of making a sale of small lots
only is that owners may rely upon their wool having full
attention of buyers who cannot be otherwise attracted by
the presence of large lots. We almost cleared our cata-
logue, thus proving our sale to be a marked success. On
Thursday, 10th inst., we will offer an attractive catalogue
of about 2000 bales, consisting of clips from New England,
Mudgee, Yass, Monaro, Goulburn, Young, and other dis-
tricts from which wool is now arriving in Sydney. Syd-
ney, 4th Jan , 1895.

A BIG BIZ!
Our Trade is Big! Our Store is Big I
Our Ideas are Bigl
We get bigger every day; on Saturday night
we are bigger than on the previous Monday,
and on the Wednesday following we are bigger
still. We can’t help growing. We have all the
elements of growth. The soil we are planted in
is good, the climate propitious, the people kind.
We are continually watered by the lifegiving
showers of a nation’s approval. Can anyone
wonder that we grow ?
Our growth is a wonderful thing. _ People
describe it as magical. There is no magic in it.
Anyone, or any set of people, can do the same if
they do as we have done. But they don’t; they
can’t find the way, charge too much for the privi-
lege of doing business with them, and generally
and deplorably make a mess of the whole affair.
That’s the mischief.
Most People Like Us! All Believe in Us!
Not a Few Swear by Us!
It’s cupboard love. We suit them, make their
money go a long way, and generally render the
path of life an easy one. There’s no sentiment
about it. It’s real biz, and no mistake. In that
way we are beloved throughout Australia, the
measure of affection each one bestows upon us
being gauged by the amount of benefit received.
The more you buy at our store the greater your
liking for us will become, because the more you
invest with us the better dividends you receive.
If living IN THE COUNTRY, send for our
Catalogue and Patterns of anything you require ;
if in town, call every day.

Government Notices.
OTICE UNDER REAL PROPERTY ACT.
APPLICATIONS having been made to bring the lands hereunder described under the provisions
of the Real Property Act, Certificates of Indefeasible Title will issue, unless Caveats be lodged in
Form B of the said Act, on or before the date named opposite each case respectively.
Diagrams delineating these lands may be inspected at the Land Titles Office, Elizabeth-street,
Sydney.
Dated at Sydney this 4th day of January, 1895, at the Registrar-General’s Department.
[l2oj CHARLES PINHEY, Registrar-General.
OTICE OF FORECLOSURE UNDER REAL PROPERTY ACT.
In the matter of the application of James Brindley Bettington and William Henry Mackenzie,
mortgagees under memorandum of mortgage from the Intercolonial Land Company (Limited),
No. 193,852, dated 10th April, 1892, over the whole of the land comprised in Certificate of Title,
vol. 576, folio 11.
WHEREAS the abovenamed mortgagees have made application for an order for foreclosure of the
mortgage above referred to, accompanied by such evidence of default for six months and other facts, as
required by section 113 of the Real Property Act: Notice is hereby given, pursuant to the said Act,
that the land comprised within the said mortgage, as hereunder particularised, is offered and will
remain open for sale until February 15, 1895, which date has been appointed as the time upon or
after which I, the Registrar-General, will issue to the applicants an order for foreclosure, which will
exclude all claim by the abovenamed mortgagor Company, and of any person claiming through or under
it, unless in the interval a sufficient amount has been realised by the sale of such land to satisfy
the principal and interest moneys due, and all costs and expenses occasioned by such proceedings, and
interest up to the time of such sale.
A Plan and further particulars may be obtained by application at the Land Titles Office, Eliza-
beth-stm t, Sydney.
Dated at Sydney this 4th day of January. 1895, at the Registrar-General’s Department.
[l2l] CHARLES PINHEY, Registrar-General.

The Sydney unemployed agitators have
decided on a new (Coxey-like) plan of campaign.
They propose to concentrate all their numbers in
Sydney, so as to “compel the Government to find
work for thein.”
The Bulletin is glad to note that at the
recent Sydney University examinations a son of
the poet Kendall got through his second year.
Young Kendall promises to become a good
Grecian—an aspiration strongly felt by his father,
who, however, lacked the opportunity and tena-
city of purpose to become a classical scholar.
Apropos of free passes, The Bulletin
would like to be informed of the circumstances
under which a certain writer was granted a first-
class free pass over all N.S.W. lines for 12 months
by the Colonial Secretary’s Department after it
had been refused him by the Railway Commis-
sioners.
Archbishop Dunn (Q.) has raised the sec-
tarian education cry. Boodlewraith is re-
joicing thereat, foreseeing a loss to the Demo-
crats, who cannot possibly compromise on the
Education question, while the gang in office will
promise anything and give—nothing !
Cabled that Harcourt has resigned the
leadership of the House of Commons, and his
place in the Government; also, that a dissolution
may be expected shortly. If this report is true,
the disagreement between Rosebery and his
colleagues must have reached a critical point.
Apparently the wound which Harcourt suffered
when Rosebery was preferred to him for the
leadership of the Liberal party has rankled instead
of healing. D spite press support, Rosebery has
not been a success as Premier; a big section of
his supporters are dissatisfied with him ; and Con-
servative prospects are steadily brightening.
Many people who read this par. must need
a galvanised tinned-iron bucket, which is a utensil
calculated to bring true joy into every home, being
useful and ornamental, and handy to put the baby
in. Those people are invited to buy that bucket
from J. Partridge, who manufactures such articles
by the million at 261 Harris-street, Pyrmont,
Sydney. Mr. Partridge also creates innumerable
galvanised tinned-iron tubs ; galvanised iron ridge-
capping, guttering, and down-pipes; colonial
ovens, and all kinds of tinware. As he has special
machinery for turning out these things promptly
and cheaply, people who deal with him will be
well served, and will have the satisfaction of
patronising a native industry and keeping their
money in the country.
The demand for Frossard’s Cavour Cigars
has lately increased so much that the capacity of
the Swiss factory has been taxed beyond its limits;
and for a short time, at least, the market supply
cannot cope with the demand. Active steps are
being taken to make the output equal to the heavy
orders from all parts of the world. The mildness
and refreshing aroma of Oavours renders them
quickly popular, and the medical profession is
recommending them in cases where the nerves or
the heart are affected by a stronger smoke. Their
low price is also in favor of Cavour cigars. This
is a result of the cheap shape adopted, which
•■nables the manufacturers to put all the value into
t le leaf employed, instead of presenting to the
p iblio a cigar with a gracefully finished point
vhieh takes a lot of time to make, and is obtained
it the expense of the quality of the leaf.

Exhibition of works
Of the
PHOTOGRAPHIC ART,
• * To be Held in the
MELBOURNE EXHIBITION GALLERIES.
From 29th JANUARY till 30th APRIL, 1895.
The Exhibition Trustees have determined to hold an
Exhibition of the Photographic Art in the Galleries con-
nected with the Aquarium, and cordially invite the
assistance and co-operation of all those who are interested
in the Photographic Art.
The Exhibition will embrace all the various branches of
the art, and will include Portraiture, Landscape, and
Scenery subjects, together with specimens of Photo-
gravure, Autotype, and all the various processes of repro-
duction of pictures from photographs, together with an
exposition of the application of Photography to Art
Industry, and Commerce.
A special section will be placed at the disposal of
amateurs for the exhibition of their work, and a separate
room will be placed at the disposal of the Amateur Photo-
graphic Association of Victoria.
The Exhibition will be open on 29th January, and
remain open till April 30. 1895. All exhibits must be
furnished with an explanatory label, legibly written or
printed.
The trustees reserve the right to reject any proffered
exhibits, and to remove any objectionable labels, &c. if
necessary.
Each exhibitor of photographs, &0., of not less than
£lO in value will be furnished with a pass for entry during
the currency of the exhibition.
All exhibits must be delivered at the Exhibition-build-
ing not later than January 19, and placed as directed by
the trustees.
Entry forms and all further particulars can be obtained
on application to the undersigned.
J. E. SHERRARD.
Secretary Exhibition Trustees.

THE FISHERMAIDEN.
(From the German.)
Thou bonnie fishermaiden,
Now row thy boat to land j
Then come and sit beside me—
We’ll talk thus, hand in hand.
Lay on my breast thy little head
And do not fearful be ;
Dost thou not daily trust thyself
To this tempestuous sea ?
My heart is just like this wild sea,
Hath storm and ebb and flow;
And in its deep full many a pearl
Of love lies far below.
Q.
P. LUMA.
Dear Bulletin, — Apropos of the eternal
strife between Woman and Religion, and the
latter’s insistence that the former should wear
more clothes, did you ever come across Reclus’
description, in “ Primitive Races,” of the manner
in which India learnt decorum at the point of the
British bayonet? He says :
The ladies of Calcutta were shocked. They represented
that Her Gracious Majesty Queen Victoria could not tole-
rate some of her subjects wearing nothing but a bead
waistband, plus a leafy bough before and behind. ...
Christianity and civilisation suppressed the innocent
nudity of the Orissa jungles. . . . In 1871, a company
of soldiers marched into a village, assembled the in-
habitants, and 19 women filed before the captain, and fell
on their knees. Two sergeants and four corporals pro-
ceeded-still in the name of Her Chaste Majesty—to per-
form the toilet of the fair sex. The first put a red mark
on the brow of the kneeling woman, to teach her the first
lesson of modesty. She rose and stepped forward to
where stripes No. 2 waited to put a hand on her shoulder,
and tear away the foliage in front—let us bow before the
virmous sovereign who presides at the drawing-rooms of
Uucki ighara Palace 1 Tommy Atkins No. 3 relieved the
savage of the foliage behind, and all the greenery was
thrown into a fire lighted for the purpose. The 4th
soldier put a petticoat on the poor woman, the sth
strapped it round her waist, and the 6th saw her out of
the door. A child of nature she went in, a civilised
woman she went out, having laid aside savagery, and
being clothed in Manchester cotton.
What a delicious joker is Mrs. Grundy !—R.

Dear Bulletin,— The 21-mile plain
stretches between the Bokhara and the Bulga, and
all the way there is not a splinter of wood big
enough to make a toothpick. Years ago, when
the'telegraph line was first erected, the wires were
supported on wooden poles, but owing to a habit
the teamsters and “swaggies ” developed of chip-
ping off enough timber to boil the billy, the wires
were always coming down, so iron posts had to be
substituted. One day, when the temperature was
about a billion in the shade, two raw newchums
were making across the 21-mile, and in their
simplicity, neglected to provide themselves with
fuel. When the billy question arose, big Ned
(who weighed about 16 stone) suggested that they
should set fire to the grass and long Patsy (the
sinewy man from Clare) should hold the billy over
the burning grass and follow it along, while he
(Ned) held the tea in readiness. There was a
nice little breeze blowing in the wrong direction
and when the grass was fired it started back at a
good brisk pace. But Patsy could run like a deer,
and he bolted after it, keeping the billy over the
burning edge, Ned bringing up the rear with a fistful
of tea. When Pat had cantered back about six miles,
the water began to boil and he looked around
anxiously for Ned, but the pace had been too
violent, and all that was visible of him was a little
cloud of dust against the sky. So Pat sat down
in the midst of that baked plain and cursed Ned
till he arrived with the tea, by which time the
water was too cool for making tea, but not cool
enough to drink. Then Ned cursed Pat for going
so fast and for not keeping the water boiling, and
then Pat cursed Ned again, and there they sat, puf-
ing and blowing and gasping with thirst, their red
eyeballs glowering at each other, and cursing
whenever they could get sufficient breath. At
last, when they had cooled down a bit, they
decided to drink the water as it was and return to
their swags. Unluckily, there are no landmarks
on the plain, and it took them the greater part of
next day to find their belongings. When at last
they succeeded, they discovered that the dingoes
had been there before them and had eaten their
provisions and destroyed things generally. Then
they cursed each other at large, after which they
refilled their billy at the clay-pan and marched on
m sorrowful and hungry silence.—Yours, G.

SOMETHING ABOUT TWO BOYS.
“The most pathetic incident of my childhood is
this : My mother had been very ill for several
weeks, and the doctor solemnly announced that
she could not live more than two or three days
longer at most. That night my father roused me
from sleep and took me out of my little bed to bid
her a last good-bye. I shall never forget the
scene, which was new and awful to me. People
were weeping all round the room, the air of which
was heavy with the odour of candles and lamps,
and reeking with the fumes of drugs. My mother
knew and kissed me, and then they took me back
to my bed. But ere I was led away some one
opened the window a few inches from the top, and
I noticed the grey dawn resting on the glass, and
heard the ‘cheCjc, cheep,’ of a newly-awakened
bird. Since then I have associated that hour and
sound with that unhappy episode.
“ But (and to say what now follows I have
written the foregoing paragraph) we were all
rasped and tortured for nothing. My mother
to get well hand-over-hand, and died
quietly thirty years afterwards. She survived
every person who stood at her bedside that night,
:except me.”
Speaking of the illness of her son, a boy of
nine, a lady says : “We bad to sit with him
night and day, giving him brandy, wine, beef-
tea, &0., to keep him alive, and expected every
■day would be his last. The physician plainly told
us that nothing more could be done to save him.”
Yet, in spite of the disease, and—we almost
said—in spite of the doctors, the lad is well to-day.
And this is how it all came about. There is a
moral in it, too, but suppose we serve that up at
the end of the story,. All right, you say. Very
well, then.
It seems that this boy, George Westmoreland,
had previously been a strong, healthy little chap,
as all boys ought to be. But about the middle of
last November—lB9l, that is—he was taken
down. Tiie family couldn’t make out what ailed
him. He complained of a bad pam in the stomach,
and vomited a quantity of yellowish-green stuff.
Presently the pain was so sharp he couldn’t lie in
bed, and they had, so his mother says, to apply
fresh hot poultices one after another. The whites
of his eyes turned yellow, and. his skin, too. He was
hot, and feverish, and had |o fight for his breath.
Of course his mother sent for a doctor, and the
doctor said his young patient was suffering from
inflammation of the; bowels. He gave medicines
which, however, did;no good. so far as the boy’s
friends could see. On the contrary, he grew
worse, and a second doctor was fetched. This
medical gentleman differed from his predecessor,
and gave out that George had an attack of rheu-
matic fever—in other words, acute rheumatism
a disease which no boy has any business with
whatever.
The treatment on this theory availed nothing ;
George was worse. He now had a hacking cough,
and his expectoration was so offensive that the
people had to use disinfectants. He broke out
into sweats, so heavy as to saturate the pillows.
He could take no nourishment save a little milk
and lime water. He wore away to a skeleton, did
the poor boy. He was nothing but skin and bone,
and they had to lift him in and out of bed. Then
he fell so ill he would not notice anyone in the
room, and lay for hours never opening his eyes.
Then came the time when a third doctor said he
couldn’t possibly live.
What happened after that the boy’s mother
tells. We give you her exact words: “In
February last,” she says, “ my husband, as a last
resource, determined to try Mother Seigel’s Cura-
tive Syrup. After a few doses the boy’s breathing
was easier and he took food. In three days he
was able to sit up, and in a week’s time he was up
and dressed. He gained flesh and strength every
day, and is now able to go about. Sometimes I
look at him and can hardly believe he is the same
boy who was so recently at death’s door. Seigel’s
Syrup saved his life. Yours truly, (signed) Mrs.
Mary Westmoreland, 5, High Street, Piumstead,
London, April 27, 1892.”
Now, half a dozen words. Little George had
no bowel inflammation, nor a single touch of rheu-
matism. That was the doctors’ professional guess-
work. He had a sharp attack of bilibusness and
indigestion, of which Mother Seigel would have
cured him long before had her medicine been
appealed to. Here is the moral to conclude with :
Learn what the true remedy for illness is, and use
it first instead of last.

At a Northern meeting to consider the
local baths question : “Gintlemen, you ’ve

Liberty (what crimes are committed in
thy name !), the promised organ of N.S.W.
National Ass, is out at last with the sweat of Death
upon its baby brow. It is conducted under the
auspices of the General Council of the Ass, con-
sisting of pretty nearly everyone who got into a
trooper’s jumper and bestrode a horse in the days
of the maritime strike. “ Jack ” Purves is there,
of course; so are Don Quixote “ Billy ” Oope,i
the ancient “Tom” Buckland and the smiling
“Tin” Kelly. Likewise, Mr, “11. De Y.
Scroggie ” and Mr. “ Montgomery-Severn ; ”
also, Mr, “Hooton-Smith ” (not to be confounded
with Shootem-Smith.) Liberty (why not also
Equality and Fraternity ?), being truly repre-
sentative of the Ass and his works, is rather dull,
but it will live as long as it is fed with a spoon.
Victoria has got news of her warship
Nelson, whereof the last previous word was that
she was hanging on to the shore by her telephone-
line. Now, however, the Defence Department
proposes to repair her if she will hold any more
nails. Against this the Customs Department
urges that, though her hull is rusty, if scraped she
would spring a leak. The reason for this rotten-
ness is that several times the caretaker went
round town and left the ship out in the rain. It
is now proposed to stop the city gutters and allow
Port Philip Bay to evaporate. The Nelson would
then rest on dry ground, and they could build a
shed over her. This would enable the crew to
sleep on board when the weather was fine, and
sometimes even when it was rather windy.
Dr. A. Mueller, of Yackandandah (Vic.),
whose strychnine treatment for snakebite is now
widely accepted as specific by a large and intel-
ligent section of the Australian medical profession,
writes as follows :
D„-ar Bulletin,—Some people are born great, others
have greatness thrust upon them. It became painfully
apparent to me that I must belong to the latter class when
I saw my old face figuring among the celebrities in your
columns. For the surreptuiouiuess with which you ob-
tained-my portrait, I can forgive you, but we shall,
serioudy quarrel if you allow Mr. “ Steele Grey,” with his
bits of Ditiu and his bits Of ill-digested knowledge, picked
up prdmis Jlously, to writs'any more nonsense in your
columns re snake-poison, strychnine and chloride of lime.
He is evidently in disguise, but through the lion’s
skin of pretended knowledge in which he envelopes
himself, I plainly see the long ears protruding!
Who ever heard of snake-poison, when injected
into the blood-stream, causing blood-clots to form,
that ft 1 up the chambers of the heart of the poison
killing in one way when thus injected and in quite another
way when injected beneath the skin ? Who does not
know by this time, if even but superficially informed, that
it is no blood poison at all, and that all presumed antidotes,
supposed to act on it chemically, after it has entered the
bloo Istream, or supposed co counteract its imaginary
blood-destruction, are simply bunkum, chloride of lime
not excepted! Mix the latter with snake-poison, or,
what means the same thing, inject it immediately
after the snake has bitten you, and in all probability
you are right ; but trust to it after the poison has
been absorbed into the system, which is effected in a
very short time, and you are sold; for snake-poison
is a proteid that differs but slightly from other
perfectly harmless proteids in the system, and the sub-
stance has yet to he found that will select the poisonous
proteids from the harmless ones with which it is mixed in
the blood. This chloride-of-lime cry was got up by a few
gentlemen who felt jealous of the success of the
strychnine treatment and would fain put it down if they
could. Finding they cannot, and, at the same time,
somewhat distrustful of their new antidote, they now
propose to divide honors by recommending both to be
used together. “Steele Grey" appears to be one of
these, or, if not, has probably rubbed his back
against their learned sides. This supposition is
coafirmjd by the concluding part of the effusion that
accompanies my portrait, and smells strongly of Alma
Mater. This “post hoe ergo propter hoe” argument has
ere this been advanced against my treatment inhalliof
science and in lecture-rooms. It unfortunately applies to
half the cures we doctors effect, but to the strychnine
treatment it does not apply—for this treatment is based
on a thoroughly scientific and well-proven theory of the
action of snake-poison, which theory led me to look upon
strychnine as the antidote to s.iake-poison, long before I
ever injected it into man or beast.—Yours truly, A.
Mueller, M.D., Yackandandah, Victoria, Deo. ’94.

Some time since, Robert Qanthony, the entertainef
(brother to Nellie of that ilk, who will soon be seen in
Australia), wrote a farcical comedy, which he gave to
Weedon Grossmith to read. Qrossmith lost the manu-
script, and was put to his wits’ end to find excuses for
not having reported on the merits of the piece. One day
he would tell Ganthony that he had not had time to read
through it, next he would praise the first act, which
was all he had so far read ; then he would affect to find
fault with the second act, and so on. At last he con-
sulted his brother George, who advised him to call at
Scotland Yard, in case the lost document might have
found its way there. When he explained his business at
J" 1 ® lost - property office, the sergeant in charge sail?
Oh yes, sir, the manuscript of a play has been found,
and as it bore the address of Mr. Ganthony, Richmond,
we returned it to him two months ago.” Then Grossmith
went away into a retired place to throw things at himself.
W. S. Gilbert has never been more brilliantly humorous
than in “ His Excellency,” writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent, but the music leaves much to be desired
It is very Sullivan-nnd-Watery. Dr, Carr intends, how
ever, to re-write several of the numbers, and doubtless
when this is done the new comic opera will be as success-
ful as any of its predecessors at the Savoy. It is beauti-
fully staged and splendidly acted. George Grossmith is
disappointing, hut that is the author’s fault as much ai
his own. John Le Hay, however, is an enormous success
and helps the piece along marvellously.

A Talk with Peel .
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Whbn in ’B2 Peel made his as an All-
EngJand representative against the Australian
Eleven at Manchester, he bowled down six
wickets for the small total of 41 runs. That
success was full of promise, and in the 12 years
that have since elapsed, Peel has proved himself
the most consistent bowler against whom Aus-
tralian batsmen have had to contend.
The famous Lohmann could do more on certain
wickets ; but on all wickets, good or bad, Peel has
ever been the same consistent Peel. Lohmann,
too, has, on a nine years’ average, beaten him for
the English bowling championship, but only by
an insignificant fraction.
Peel stands alone as the English bowler who in
county matches for the last 12 seasons has sent
over 1000 batsmen back to the pavilion. It is
Peel who has done sensational bowling in Aus-
tralia, bagging, in ’B4, 353 wickets for 5 - 75 runs a
wicket. At Moss Vale he got 18 wickets for
seven runs ! He distinguished himself, too, with
Lord Sheffield’s eleven, coming out with a batting
average of 39 for all matches, and taking second
place in bowling- records. His highest score is 226
not out for Yorkshire against Leicestershire.
He weighed lOst. 71 b. when he first left Eng-
land with the ’B4 team for Australia; his weight
increased to list. 71b. on the voyage, and he
has never since scaled less than list. He looks
now a pocket Hercules, scales a solid list. 41b.
and stands sft. Gin.
This visit to Australia being his fourth, he
speaks authoritatively when he remarks the won-
derful improvement that the last 10 years have
made in Australian cricket. Here is the substance
of what he said in Sydney the week before last.
“There are 10 cricketers now, in the true sense
of the word, where there was but one in ’B4. Then
we played 15 of South Australia; now out* team
goes down before their eleven. ■ Gregory has come
to the front in the last few years, possessing more
strokes than any other batsman, English or Aus-
tralian. Iredale, too, who bats with an expert’s
skill and vigor, and particularly cuts with a dash
which would rank him a great gun in England.
Then George Giffen, safer than Gregory, though
with fewer strokes ; and withal the champion all-
round cricketer. Turner, also, who has risen to
top eminence as a bowler, being underrated
as a batsman in consequence. And what
great development there has been in Australian
country cricketers ! Game as pebbles, they will
field anything, and will try to catch a cannon-shot'.
Country batsmen force the game, and though none
promise great things, many are very fair bats
indeed. They hit too; they will knock the cover
off soft bowling. I remark, also, that nearly all
the country bowlers make the ball turn well, keep
cool and use their heads. A. B. Trott, of Mel-
bourne, I should select as possibly the most
promising junior.
“There is a marked contrast between English
and Australian batsmen, and considerable dif-
ference between the batsmen of the several
Austral provinces. The Melbourne batsmen cut
more than the Adelaide men, the Sydney men
still more, but the Englishmen are beyond them
all. The Adelaide men have a cramped style,
they never reach out and smother the ball. Theirs
is a style which is safe enough on their fast and
splendid wicket. But I send down balls on the
off to them with impunity, which if bowled to
Stoddart or Iredale would be sent with a swinging
cut like lightning to the boundary. Against
Englishmen, I bowl more on the wicket than I do
against Sydney batsmen, and more on the wicket
against Sydney batsmen than against the batsmen
of the other provinces. I need not say that two
or three Sydney batsmen poke about the wicket
and play a slow game.
“ Among the Brisbane cricketers I should pick
i l * l or a P^ ace any Australian eleven.
iJr. Macdonald can play nicely, but he belongs to
the class of A. Bannerman, Dr. Barrett, and the
Englishman, Barlow.
“An English wicket is very different from an
Australian, and Australian wickets differ from
one another. That of Adelaide is the fastest in
the world ; next come Melbourne and Brisbane,
then Sydney, and compared with Sydney wicket
our English ones are quite slow. The ball
will break no more on the best Australian
pitch than on English ones; but in wet
weather, the ball lodging on the “home”
wickets cuts through,” as the cricket phrase is ;
that is, it chips out a piece of turf. The Aus-
tralian wicket will not chip after rain; but it
becomes sticky, and doesn’t it turn the ball 1 Ask
-turner. When an English wicket is bad it is
more fiery and kicks more than an Australian
wicket, but it turns the ball less. I pitch the ball
shorter here than I do in England, to keep a
good length. All things considered, Australian
Wickets are batsmen’s wickets. Of all cricket-
grounds in the world the Sydney ground is the
prettiest, and greener than any other in Aus-
tralia. It is more suggestive of the deep, rich
greenness of the English turf.”
Thus Peel talked, as he rested, the Sunday
morning after the first two days of the test match.
Like the rest of the Englishmen he had many
words of generous praise for the grand score of
Australians, and thought their chance was
then 100. to 1. Gregory’s innings, he says, is the
feature of the present-tour.
“ 586! I was never so tired as on
Saturday afternoon. When we came in from the
field at the close of that big innings, I was too
done-up to take my boots off. And then three of
our wickets fell for a mere song, and at quarter
past five 1 had to get ready to go in. So I put on
my pads, and stretching myself on a seat in the
dressing-room instantly fell asleep. I cannot
describe how glad I was when Lockwood woke me
at five minutes to six with the welcome news that
I would not be wanted that night. I had bowled
53 overs, and had put every ounce of my strength
into every ball I sent down, and had fielded under
that hot sun my very best. I think the turn of
the game came when we missed the easy chance at
the beginning of Iredale’s innings of running him
or Giffen out. I have often noticed that when a
batsman has a big ‘ let off ’ he invariably makes a
great stand.

The Age concludes an article on the pro-
mises of modern science with some very solemn
reflections, of which this is the gist : What is to
hinder electricity from doing the bidding of some
future anarchist, and blowing an effete civilisation
sky-high ?” Which recalls Charles Lamb’s horror
of matches, and that 11 hellish faculty above gun-
powder” they gave into the hands of the lawless
hind. “Think,” he wrote, “of a disrespected
clod, that was trod into earth, on a sudden by
damned arts refined into an exterminating angel,
devouring the fruits of the earth and their growers
in a mass of fire.” And yet matches are now a
penny a thousand, and the world remains.
When buying The Bulletin at any wharf
or railway-station, make sure, before paying,
that you get the current issue, as a number of
people h°ve been making money by trading off
ancient copies on the unsuspecting.
At Ashfield (Sydney), on Christinas night,
a rejected lover sang “ I’ve just come away from
the inquest,” prior to shooting a bullet into his
heart. The world knows nothing of its greatest
humorists until they are dead.
At a Northern sugar-mill, recently, a
Kanaka came in from the field with a load of cane,
and seeing it was only five minutes to six decided to
hang round the mill till the whistle blew for
knock-off. In the meantime the owner came and
shifted the hands of the clock back. When
Tommy came again to see the time it was only
5.30. Taking the clock the ex-cannibal went
round to the fireman and requested him to open
the furnace-dopr. “What for?” asked the man,
as he complied. “ Burn this up ;no good ;
go backwards,” replied the savage as he threw the
clock into the fire. The erring heathen was
promptly sacked by the indignant proprietor,
who, however, was obliged to send for the local
“ foorce ” to remove him from the premises.
The latest from ’Ome is “ Ballarat Bill,”
by Bracebridge Fleming, a book of the “Dead-
wxid Dick” series,’' now rapidly selling in Aus-
tralia. The scene is laid at Ballarat; the time,
this year. Ballarat is described as being princi-
pally composed of saloons and cowboy toughs
armed with revolvers and bowies—the Australian
practice being, it is said, to carry two of the latter,
one in the belt, the other concealed in the back.
Gold is described as being picked up in lumps in
the bush surrounding the place, and kangaroos
and bushrangers are plentiful within a stone’s
throw. The bushrangers are supplied with
Maxim guns. The outlaws are, of course,
badly whipped, and the very-much-compromised
females rescued. Camping is described as “out-
spanning,” and travelling through the bush is des-
cribed as “trekking”—South African terms.
Absurdities like these and worse abound, but
“ Ballarat Bill ” sells like hot cakes, while locally-
printed books lie mourning on the shelves.
Dear Bulletin, —The “ Song of Father
Duchesne ” reminds me of a ditty I heard in Tas-
mania 14 or 15 years ago. The old fellow who
sang it told me afterwards that it was “as old as
God,” a statement I remember to have doubted.
It is supposed to be sung in a condemned cell by a
prisoner who is very soon to be hanged. The
earlier verses I forget, and remember only that
the sentiments were similar to Father Duchesne’s,
with a few others quite unmentionable. The
concluding verses run this way
The parson he will come, he will come, he will come,
( Repeat )
A digger who returned to Melbourne per
s.s. Innamincka to spend his Xrnas with his
family, from whom he had been absent two years,
relates that, while in the wilderness beyond Cue,
he used to leave a dish of water outside his
tent night and day for the. vagrant birds. He had
often to cart this same water 20 and 30 miles by
camels, but ho had his reward, for the little birds
grew so grateful that they perched on his shoulder
and ncalled in his hands.
Dear Bulletin, —That “record” team-
load fpr one. waggon has been beaten, several
times. In 1890, W. Bonfiejd conveyed 13 tons
15 owt. from the Overflow station to Nyngan (80
miles) in four days, with only 11 horses. In 1893,
C. Johnston delivered in Cowra 14 tons 10 cwt.,
drawn by 24 bullocks. I could mention at least a
dozen more that have gone close to 13 tons, but
the largest load of wOol I ever saw on one waggon
came into Bourke last year. The waggon con-
tained 110 bales—weight 13 tons 4 cwt., from
Tlmrlagoona, Q. — Thomas M'Guikh.
A Victorian country commission agent
lately did some business for a farmer, noted for
his splendid herd of cattle, and was induced to
take “ a fine cow ” in lieu of the commission. The
cow was duly forwarded, and a day or two later
the following letter of acknowledgment came to
hand :
Dear Sir,—Your cow of the 4th inst. reached me to-day,
and I have to thank you for sending such a fine animal.
You said that if I left it to you you would pick out one
specially for me—and you have done so. If it were
quieter and in better condition and gave more milk and
had two eyes instead of one, it might suit me better, but,
I suppose, we cannot have everything we want in this
world. Or if it had been totally blind, one boy might
be able to get it in and milk it ; or else I could
send it on to the Blind Asylum: but, as it is,
I am afraid they would not take it in there. The chief
points about the cow appear to be that it travels well, and
goes over—or through—fences in grand style, and is
always uncertain as to where it is going to. As a speci-
men of your famous herd it has attracted some attention
here. A farmer in the' district' who intends breeding a
superior class of, cattle and was going to see you and
inspect your young stock, took particular notice of it to-
day, but I don’t know whether he will call on you now. I
am afraid, however, that the cow is too valuable and too
well-bred for me, so I shall be glad if you will send for it.
You might take it back and forward me a cheque for
whatever you think it is worth, and I will get a common,
ordinary cow—one that will stand quietly and be milked
and can be caught without the aid of a stockman and
horse. lam not educated up to these high-class animals.
‘—Yours truly, —-.
Dear Bulletin, —It daily grows harder
for the hona-jide bushman to find work in the
country. Besides legions of Melbourne, Ade-
laide and Sydney unemployed, even ex-oivil-
servants are beginning to take his place. Amongst
the men working at a woolscour on the Merri-
Merri there are an ex-post-and-telegraph master,
a railway-guard, a former rabbit-inspector, a
schoolmaster, a bank-accountant, a commission-
agent, and a B.A. of Dublin University ; the
trades of typesetting, bootmaking, painting and
tailoring are also represented. It is enough to
make one wish he was never born when, after
walking through miles of swampy, black-soil
country up to his knees in water, or across a dry,
dead plain under a broiling sun and tormented by
millions of flies, he arrives at a Woolshed half-
dead from hunger or thirst, only to find that a
gang of boxer-hat-wearing, broken-down city men,
who have never seen a sheep shorn, or a tree
rung, or known a day’s real hardship, have gob in
ahead of him by train from Sydney.—6 xB.
The parson he will come and look so horrid glum
And talk of Kingdom Gome—damn his eyes!
The sheriff he’ll come too, he’ll come too, he’ll come too,
( Repeat)
The sheriff he’ll come too, with all his ghastly crew,
His dirty work to do—damn his eyes!
The crowd, O hear them yell, hear them yell, hear them
yell,
(Repeat)
The crowd, O hear them yell, above that cursed bell,
May I meet them all in hell—damn their eyes 1
Toowoomba (Q.).
W. G. B.
